Mass Transfer from Nano-Bubbles for Anti-Bacterial Applications

Supervisors:
Philip Loldrup Fosbøl

==PAGE:4==
I
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Water is a vital resource important to take good care of, especially with the possibil-
ities of recycling. Nowadays, wastewater worldwide contains an increasing amount of
environmentally harmful substances and pathogens. Many of such contaminants are
removed in wastewater treatment processes using harsh chemicals such as chlorine or
chlorine dioxide. It is the vision of the Danish company Norlex to reduce the exten-
sive use of harsh chemicals in wastewater treatment by using treatment with ozone
micro- and/or nano-bubbles. Ozone is a strong oxidizing agent, which also posses anti-
bacterial and anti-viral properties. However, the eﬃciency of mass transfer into water
is limited for ozone. Micro- and nano-bubbles have long residence times in water, they
represent a larger surface-to-volume ratio, and they have a high internal pressure -
all of which should enhance the mass transfer of ozone into water. Norlex wishes to
get a deeper understanding of the ozone transfer via bubbles to the bulk liquid. This
should be obtained by developing mathematical mass-transfer models and by carrying
out experiments.
In this project ﬁve diﬀerent mathematical models simulating the ozone diﬀusion from
a bubble were developed and implemented in MatLab. The models were analysed and
compared in order to select the best model for describing the mass transfer process.
The best model turned out to be a non-linear single-ﬁlm model including only the
liquid ﬁlm and accounting for time dependent factors such as: 1) the decreasing ozone
concentration inside the bulk gas phase, and 2) the bubble shrinkage. Bubble shrinkage
was predicted to increase the ﬂux and the degree of non-linearity thereby providing a
faster mass transfer. The presence of inert gases would slow down the diﬀusion process.
A solid experimental set-up was established for generating tiny bubbles containing
ozone (using a decompression method). The average bubble size of the resulting bub-
bles was determined using a particle size analyser. The average size of the bubbles was
about 50µm. This means that the bubbles generated were ”micro-bubbles”. Simple
Sherwood correlations predicted that the liquid ﬁlm thickness for a stagnant bubble
was half the bubble diameter.
The lifetime of a 50µm bubble was calculated to be 136ms.
The time required to
complete the mass transfer through the liquid ﬁlm was 306ms. Due to their rapid mass
transfer the bubbles have a great potential for being used in disinfection processes at
wastewater treatment plants.

Preface
This master thesis of 30 ECTS points is written under the Department of Chemical
and Biochemical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in coop-
eration with the Danish company Norlex. The thesis is written over a period from the
16th of February to the 16th of July 2018.
First of all I would like to thank my supervisor, associate professor Philip Loldrup
Fosbøl, from the Center of Energy Resources Engineering (CERE) under the Depart-
ment of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering. During the entire project work he was
very encouraging both academically as well as personally. He always provided excellent
and inspiring advise and guidance.
I would also like to thank the company Norlex for the collaboration throughout my
project. I am very grateful for getting an insight into their company and to be able to
work with an industrial problem of mass transfer. I would like to give a special thanks
to the company representative, Jesper Sanderbo. He helped me to gain a better under-
standing of the entire wastewater treatment process and the possibilities of applying
ozone rather than other harsher chemicals in this process. Furthermore, he showed me
how to experimentally generate the bubbles and measure their sizes.
Lyngby, July 2018

==PAGE:13==
Project Description
3.1
Background
Environmentally harmful substances are represented by a large group of diﬀerent
compounds, mostly man-made, and do not naturally occur in nature in measurable
amounts. Generally, these substances are poisonous to living organisms and diﬃcult
to break down in nature.[1] Wastewater contains an increasing number of such com-
pounds, which can be quite a challenge to remove especially without the use of harsh
chemicals. Furthermore, wastewater contains bacteria and other pathogens, which can
cause disease and pose a risk to human health. Usually today, the pathogens are inac-
tivated or eliminated by chemicals such as chlorine or chlorine dioxide.
Many wastewater treatment plants around the globe strive to remove environmentally
harmful substances and pathogens as eﬀectively, resource optimized, and environmen-
tally friendly as possible.
Norlex is a company which main objective is to provide sustainable solutions and
wastewater technologies for reducing the use of chemicals in the treatment process.
Norlex is in the process of developing an ozone generator to produce active ozone bub-
bles of sizes of micro- or nano-scale to replace other chemicals. Some researchers and
scientists claim that by reducing the bubble size to micro- or nano-scale the mass trans-
fer eﬃciency will be higher and the dissolution of ozone will be signiﬁcantly enhanced.
However, generally speaking these claims are are not adequately scientiﬁcally, techni-
cally, and mathematically documented. Only a small amount of experimental work
on micro- and nano-bubbles has been carried out by an internal group of Japanese
scientists, but not really well documented. Norlex needs to carefully investigate the
eﬀects of nano- and micro-bubbles regarding gas transfer to liquids in order to provide
better wastewater solutions.
3.2
Purpose
The overall purpose of this project is to contribute knowledge to Norlex about the
underlying principles of their ozone bubble generator. This includes to investigate and
document existing knowledge regarding ozonation of wastewater and the advancing
technology of micro- and nano-bubbles. Furthermore, Norlex wants to gain a more
profound understanding of gas transfer to a liquid phase using bubbles.
Best is if
this understanding can also be achieved through mathematical models.
Generally,
1
==PAGE:14==
3.2. PURPOSE
CHAPTER 3. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
mass transfer models will enable Norlex to optimize and further develop their ozone
generator more easily.
Norlex is also questioning whether their prototype ozone generator produces bubbles
within the right size interval, in order to be characterised as micro- or nano-bubbles.
So Norlex also requests an experimental determination of the bubble size distribution.
3.2.1
Outline of Speciﬁc Objectives
• Review existing knowledge of:
– most common wastewater treatments and ozonation processes
– the anti-bacterial applications of ozone in treatments plants
– micro- and nano-bubbles
– mass transfer of gas to a liquid phase
• Develop complex non-linear mathematical models describing the mass transfer of
ozone from a bubble to the surrounding ﬂuid.
• Develop simple linear models based on mass transfer coeﬃcients.
• Analyse the complex models individually. Clearly state the possibilities and the
limitations of the various mathematical models.
• Make a thorough sensitivity analysis of the models and state the parameters that
aﬀect the diﬀusion process the most.
• Evaluate whether a basic linear model can provide an equally good description of
the mass transfer process as the complex discretized models.
• Compare all the mathematical models and determine all model input parameters
in order to select the most suitable mathematical set-up for describing the mass
transfer.
• Clearly describe the experimental equipment used to generate tiny bubbles, and
explain the physical principles behind the bubble formation.
• Establish experimental limitations for measuring the bubble sizes using a laser
diﬀraction particle size analyser.
• If possible experimentally determine the average bubble size.
• Report and present ﬁndings.
2
==PAGE:15==
Main Project Parts
The project will include the following parts in order to meet the requirements of the
project description:
4.1
Literature Survey
In order to establish a basic understanding a thorough literature study will be carried
out. This chapter in the report will include a brief description of Norlex as a com-
pany and an introduction to the general stages of normal wastewater treatment. Then
descriptions of possible ozone applications in these treatment processes will be pre-
sented. Emphasis will be put on the anti-bacterial and anti-viral properties of ozone.
Then several aspects of the innovative technology of micro- and nano-bubbles will be
described. Finally, the theoretical foundation of diﬀusion and mass transfer will be
described especially focusing on ﬁlm theories such as ”The Two-Film Theory”, ”The
Penetration Theory”, and so on.
4.2
Model Development
Five diﬀerent mathematical models will be developed. First a highly simpliﬁed model
will be developed assuming an inﬁnite supply of ozone from the bulk gas phase of the
bubble. Furthermore, the model will not account for volume changes. This basic model
(Model 1) will be transformed into an appropriate matrix-form of ordinary diﬀerential
equations.
This basic model will be further developed into two new mathematical
models.
Model 2 will take into account that the amount of ozone inside the bulk
gas phase decreases during the diﬀusion process. Model 3 will additionally account
for volume changes throughout the diﬀusion process. This will be accomplished by
establishing a volume-based discretization scheme. Finally, two mathematical model
based on mass transfer coeﬃcients will be developed. These models will mathematically
be more simple and provide a linear description of the diﬀusion process. The ﬁrst of
these linear models will not account for volume changes, whereas the second model
will.
All models will be implemented in MatLab and solved either numerically or
analytically.
3
==PAGE:16==
4.3. MODEL ANALYSIS
CHAPTER 4. MAIN PROJECT PARTS
4.3
Model Analysis
The model analysis will include both mathematical and numerical methods. The ef-
fects of the input parameters on the model outputs will be analysed through detailed
sensitivity analyses.
The models will also be analysed by studying: 1) the ozone
concentration as a function of time at diﬀerent radial positions, and 2) the ozone con-
centration as a function of the radius at diﬀerent moments in time. These parts of
the analysis shall indicate whether the models adhere to the general principles of the
diﬀusion theory. Speciﬁc model features will also be studied in greater detail. It is
e.g. important to check whether the volume change feature is implemented correctly
in Model 3.
Along the way the three complex mathematical models will be compared to each other
graphically and through the sensitivity analyses. Finally, the simple linear models will
be compared to Model 2 and Model 3. This should answer the question whether a
simple model can lead to equally relevant conclusions as a complex model.
4.4
Experimental Work
The experimental work will be divided into two diﬀerent parts: 1) the generation of
micro- and/or nano-bubbles using Norlex’s experimental set-up, and 2) the measure-
ment of the bubble sizes.
Norlex applies a decompression method in the generation of the bubbles. The exper-
imental set-up will be described in detail and so will the reasons for applying this
decompression method.
The chemical reagent N,N-diethyl-p-phenyldiamine will be
used to react with ozone in order to demonstrate the presence of ozone in the water.
A particle size analyser will be used to measure the bubble size distribution a parti-
cle size analyser will be used. In order to test whether the particle size analyser can
measure the size of bubbles, measurements will performed on deionized water both
with and without bubbles. Then these volume distribution curves will be analysed and
compared. A brief statistical analysis will be included. Here the average bubble size
will be calculated as well as the standard deviation and the coeﬃcient of variance.
The experiments will be conducted at DTU, Department of Chemical and Biochemical
Engineering, in cooperation with Jesper Sanderbo from Norlex.
All the results will be documented and thoroughly analysed in order to reach ﬁnal
conclusions.
A Gantt chart, which shows how the work has progressed in the ﬁve
months period can be seen in Appendix Y.
4
==PAGE:17==
Introduction
The global population is steadily increasing. The water resources are limited and get-
ting exhausted. Only 2.5% of water on Earth is freshwater, of which over 2
3 is still
frozen in glaciers and in polar ice caps.[2] In many developing countries they are not
only suﬀering from water shortage but also heavy water contamination.[3] Freshwa-
ter can be a renewable source if treated properly in wastewater plants. So there is
a great demand for developing inexpensive, eﬀective, and rapid wastewater treatment
techniques. Due to global environmental considerations the new treatment solutions
should also be environmentally friendly and free from the addition of harsh chemicals.
The disinfection step in wastewater treatments is essential in removing bacteria and
other pathogens causing diseases. In this step many harsher chemicals like chlorine, or
bromine are used today. These chemicals can potentially form by-products which in
certain concentrations can be hazardous to human health. Norlex is in the process of
developing eﬀective replacements for these harsh chemicals. This is in the form of an
ozone generator for the generation of active ozone micro- and nano-bubbles.
Because of their small sizes micro- and nano-bubbles should have properties diﬀerent
from ordinary bubbles. The generation of micro- and nano-bubbles is an innovative
technology which originates from Japan. The concept is still in the initial stages of
research. Many Japanese scientists already see it as a miracle product. There is even
established a ”Fine Bubble Industries Association” dedicated to accelerate the devel-
opment of the micro- and nano-bubble industry.[4] According to Japanese research it
should be advantageous to apply the remarkably small bubbles within many scientiﬁc
and technological ﬁelds such as water treatment, biomedical engineering, and nano-
materials.[5] The smaller bubbles will provide larger speciﬁc surface areas and higher
inner pressures, which should result in higher mass transfer eﬃciency.[6] So micro- and
nano-bubbles should be able to enhance the mass transfer of ozone and also be able to
continuously provide ozone to wastewater.
Before developing the ozone generator any further Norlex has to make sure that the
micro- and nano-bubbles really do make a diﬀerence. This can i.a. be determined by
the development of mathematical diﬀusion models. Norlex must also prove that it is
possible experimentally to generate tiny bubbles.
5
==PAGE:18==
Literature Survey
In this chapter all knowledge obtained from literature is reviewed. This chapter pri-
marily serves to give a comprehensive understanding of the foundation of the project.
This background knowledge is important in order to understand the reasons for Norlex
to develop an ozone generator using micro-/nano-bubbles and the problems related to
the product development. The literature review is divided into six parts. The project
is based on research and development at Norlex. So the ﬁrst part of the literature
review is dedicated to describing the company’s mission and vision as well as their cur-
rent technologies for treating wastewater. This section is based on information from
company brochures. The following parts include a brief overview of the cleaning stages
in wastewater treatments, a description of ozonation processes. It also contains an
introduction to the Japanese research on nano-bubbles, and a review of general theory
of mass transfer from bubbles. Some of the fundamental knowledge regarding mass
transfer becomes important in the more analytical parts of the project. It is essen-
tial when developing the mathematical and when evaluating the simulations in greater
detail.
6.1
Norlex’s Mission and Vision
The mission of Norlex is to provide value propositions which satisfy current environ-
mental legislations and is able to be easily updated and customized to meet future
environmental requirements. The company aims at designing and producing solutions
which as eﬀectively as possible cleans the wastewater. It is a value-driven company
focusing on providing premium value propositions, and a high degree of personalized
service. The vision of the company is to be a forerunner in developing sustainable and
environmentally friendly technologies for water treatments.
Norlex is a privately owned company founded in 2011. It is organized in two separate
product divisions.[7] The ﬁrst division, Norlex Chemicals A/S, focuses on providing
chemicals to wastewater treatment and cleaning of membranes. They oﬀer precipitant
chemicals to optimize coagulation processes, ﬂocculation agents, polymers (both an-
ionic, nonionic, and cationic), antifoaming agents, and chemicals to decompose H2S.[8]
The second division, Norlex Systems APS, designs and produces complete systems and
components for the treatment of wastewater. They i.a. produce components such as
screen ﬁlters, sand ﬁlters, and dosing systems.[9] Actually, Norlex is a market leader in
the Nordic region within the ﬁelds of cleaning chemicals and water treatment solutions.
Norlex eﬃciently utilizes existing technologies but also innovates and develops brand
6
==PAGE:19==
6.2. WASTEWATER TREATMENT
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
new technology approaches. [7]
In the future there will undoubtedly be more restrictive legislation regarding wastew-
ater puriﬁcation and discharge in Denmark, Europe, and globally. The wastewater
should be cleaned for more and more environmentally hazardous substances including
diethylhexyl phthalate (DEHP) and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs).[9] This
gives Norlex good opportunities to expand market on a global scale.
Before proceeding to the reasons why ozone is such a good and active chemical in
wastewater treatments some general theory about the composition of usual wastewater
streams are presented. Moreover, the next section also presents the general stages of
puriﬁcation in wastewater treatments.
6.2
Wastewater Treatment
Wastewater treatment is an extremely complex process. Many factors will inﬂuence
the treatment process and the plant design such as: 1) the type and amount of eﬄuent,
2) the type of collection systems available, 3) the climate from Arctic to tropical, 4) the
level of civilisation, 5) the skill level of the maintenance staﬀ, and 6) the investment
and maintenance budgets available. Wastewater treatment plants around the world
are built in a number of diﬀerent ways and include various unit operations. Several
wastewater treatment technologies are used globally and all the technologies have their
advantages and disadvantages in terms of construction costs, operational costs, energy
consumption, operational complexity, eﬄuent quality, and environmental impact.[10]
The description of a typical plant design in the next section will be based on a typical
European wastewater system.
Several pollutants are present in wastewater. The type of pollutants in the water de-
pends on where the wastewater is generated - industrial, agricultural, or municipal.
The pollutants can be categorized as inorganic, organic, and biological. The inorganic
pollutants are mainly heavy metals, which have toxic and carcinogenic eﬀects.[11] Other
inorganic contaminants are nitrates, phosphates, sulphates, and oxolates. These com-
pounds can also have serious hazardous eﬀects. The most common organic pollutants
include PAHs, pesticides, phenols, halogenated aromatic hydrocarbons, detergents,
oils, alcohols, and pharmaceuticals. The biological pollutants are microbes which may
be responsible for various types of diseases. The infectious microbes include bacteria,
fungi, algae, plankton, and viruses. The wastewater pollutants are either in solvated,
colloidal or in suspended form.[12, 13] Even though many contaminants are present
in wastewater toxicity is only observed above certain permissible limits.[14] When the
wastewater is collected and send to a treatment plant it usually contains 99.9 wt%
water and 0.1 wt% of other materials (suspended, colloidal or dissolved).[15] The com-
position of physical, chemical, and biological constituents present in the wastewater are
important parameters in the design and operation of wastewater plants. The composi-
tion of the untreated wastewater and the subsequently treated eﬄuent will e.g. depend
on the composition of the municipal water supply, the number and type of industrial
7
==PAGE:20==
6.2. WASTEWATER TREATMENT
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
establishments, and the nature of the residential community. So the composition of
wastewater varies greatly from area to area.[15] The wastewater have to go through
four stages of treatment to attain a very high eﬄuent quality. The four treatment
stages are illustrated in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: The four stages of wastewater treatment.
The four stages are brieﬂy described in order to get a good overview of the entire
wastewater treatment process.
Preliminary Treatment
The purpose of the preliminary treatment is to protect and economize the operation
of the wastewater plant. When entering the treatment plant the wastewater contains
large solids and grit which is washed into the sewer lines by rainwater. This can inter-
fere with the subsequent treatment processes and cause unnecessary process problems,
mechanical wear, and increased maintenance on treatment equipment. Large objects
like cotton buds, plastics, diapers, rags, sanitary items, face wipers, and bottle tops can
easily clog the downstream equipment, piping, and appurtenances [16, 17, 18] So these
objects must be removed in a preliminary treatment step. The preliminary treatment
prior to entering the treatment plant is also required to protect the health of workers,
protect the environment, and ensure safe disposal of sludge.[19] The preliminary treat-
ment is mainly a mechanical treatment and can include the following unit operations:
screening, grit removal, septage handling, odour control, and ﬂow equalization.[20]
Usually, screening is the ﬁrst unit operation used at treatment plants.[17] The solid
wastes removed from the wastewater are disposed oﬀin landﬁlls or sent to combustion
stations.[16]
Primary Treatment
In the primary treatment material that either ﬂoat or settle readily due to gravity is
removed. The wastewater is temporarily kept in a tank in which the heavier solids
can settle while lighter materials can ﬂoat to the surface.[21, 22] In this process sed-
imentation immediately follows ﬂocculation, and the ﬂocs continue to form and grow
during the entire sedimentation process. The addition of ﬂocculent agents greatly en-
hances the ﬂocculation and makes the sedimentation happen faster.[23] The settled
solids (primary sludge) are removed by mechanical scrapers and pushed to the cen-
ter of the tanks. Later the primary sludge is pumped out of the tanks into gravity
thickeners for a dewatering process.[24] Mechanical surface-skimming devices remove
grease and other ﬂoating materials.[25] The grease and scum are blended with the grit
and screenings from the preliminary treatment.[24] An eﬀective primary treatment can
greatly reduce the costs of the secondary and tertiary treatments.
8
==PAGE:21==
6.2. WASTEWATER TREATMENT
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
Secondary Treatment
The secondary treatment is a biological treatment. Here the wastewater is treated us-
ing a wide range of microorganisms, mainly bacteria.[26] Bacteria are especially useful
because they have a broad spectrum and are capable of cleaning complex contamina-
tion. Some bacteria are highly specialized and can convert special contaminants.[27]
The micro-organisms will convert biodegradable matter into simple substances and
biomass. This decomposition of organic matter occurs through two diﬀerent processes:
1) by biological oxidation, and 2) by biosynthesis. Through the process of biological
oxidation some end-products like minerals are formed. These products remain in so-
lution and are discharged with the eﬄuent. The oxidation process follows this general
reaction:
COHNS
Organic matter
+
Bacteria
+
O2
NH3
+Energy
+
CO2
+
End products
On the other hand the biosynthesis will incorporate the organic matter into new cells.
This forms a dense biomass which can be removed by sedimentation or another removal
process. The biosynthesis follows this reaction scheme:
COHNS
Organic matter
+
Bacteria
+
O2
C5H7NO2
New Cells
The biological treatment can both occur under aerobic or anaerobic conditions.[28] In
many cases microorganisms are able to break down environmentally hazardous sub-
stances without being damaged.[27]
Tertiary Treatment
The tertiary treatment is a chemical treatment. In the processes of this treatment step
chemicals will react with a portion of the undesired chemicals and heavy metals in the
water. The aim is to remove as many toxic compounds as possible. In the tertiary
treatment up to 99% of all pollutants can be removed depending on the safe quality
the water should attain.[12] The usual chemical processes involve: chemical precipi-
tation, neutralization, adsorption, disinfection, and ion exchange. After the primary
and secondary treatment processes there are still some pathogens remaining in the
wastewater. The disinfection of wastewater is the last step of the tertiary treatment
process. In this step a disinfectant is added to the eﬄuent in order to eliminate or at
least inactivate pathogens. Here the water will be puriﬁed up to the extend that it can
no longer cause infective diseases in humans, animals, or plants that come into contact
with the water.[29] Common disinfectants are: chlorine, chlorine dioxide, ultraviolet
radiation, and bromine.[28, 30]
In the next section possible applications of ozone in the primary, secondary, and tertiary
wastewater treatments will be presented.
9
==PAGE:22==
6.3. OZONATION
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
6.3
Ozonation
Ozone (O3) is a slightly bluish gas with a purgent odour. It consists of three oxygen
atoms in an angular conﬁguration with a bond angle of 116.8°.[31] It is an unstable
molecule, formed from an exothermic reaction between oxygen atoms. The heat of
formation of ozone is 142.85 J/mole. Ozone is a strong oxidizing agent; it is 1.5 times
stronger than chlorine. Actually, it is the third strongest oxidant overall with a redox
potential of 2.07V.[32, 11] Other important chemical properties of ozone are listed in
Appendix A.
There are several other reasons why ozone is a potential useful chemical in the treatment
of wastewater and drinking water. Foremost ozone has the ability to oxidise a wide
range of wastewater pollutants:
• Ozone oxidises inorganic materials like iron and manganese ions.[11]
• Ozone oxidises manganese complexes.[11]
• Ozone can oxidise organic compounds such as pesticides, phenols, and detergents.[11]
• Ozone oxidises cyanides, nitrites, and sulphides thereby rendering them safer and
biodegradable.[11]
• Given proper contact times and doses ozonation can increase the biodegradability
and bio-availability of PAHs.[33, 34]
• Ozone can eﬀectively oxidise diethyl phthalate (DEP).[35]
• Ozone oxidises compounds which bring about various tastes, smells, and colours
in water.[11]
Ozone is also a good ﬂocculent agent for many small dissolved organics and it generally
helps reducing the turbidity by ﬂocculation and precipitation. [11]
All of the above properties of ozone makes it applicable in many stages of the wastew-
ater treatment. It is useful as a pre-treatment in for several stages of water treatment
procedures (for pre-oxidation). It can also be used for ”intermediate oxidations” as
well as in the ﬁnal disinfection process.[36] Below ideal ozone applications are shown:
1) prior to secondary treatment, and 2) in the tertiary treatment.
Ozone Addition Prior to the Secondary Treatment
Ozone can be applied prior to the secondary treatment. This is because ozone improves
the level of biodegradability in wastewaters. Ozone partially oxideses organic matter
to give low molecular weight oxygenated compounds rather than complete oxidation to
carbon dioxide.[36] Ozone dissolved in water can react with the organic compounds in
two diﬀerent ways either by direct oxidation as ozone molecule or by indirect reaction
through formation of secondary oxidants like hydroxyl radicals.[37]
The chemical oxygen demand (COD) is a measure of water and wastewater quality.
The COD is a measure of the amount of oxygen needed to chemically oxidise organic
10
==PAGE:23==
6.3. OZONATION
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
water contaminants to inorganic end products. If the wastewater is preozonated, the
biological oxidation step results in a COD reduction of about 83% at 35°C. If only the
biological oxidation (the secondary treatment) is carried out without a preozonation
step the COD reduction is only 55%.[36] So the COD is reduced by 28% percentage
points more if ozone is used in a preozonation step. Other studies by Siddiqiui et
al. (1997) showed that if preozonation is applied prior to using a biological ﬁlter 40%
to 60% of the dissolved organic matter (DOC) is removed. When the bio-ﬁlter was
used without pre-treatment the reduction of DOC was only 13%.[38] So the beneﬁ-
cial eﬀects of preozonation is clear. The biodegradability is strongly aﬀected by the
ozone dose applied in the preozonation step. Figure 6.2 shows the biodegradability
measured as the BOD/COD ratio against the ozone concentration in g/L. BOD is the
’Biochemical Oxygen Demand’ and it describes the amount of dissolved oxygen needed
by aerobic biological organisms to break down organic material. The higher the BOD
value relative to COD, the more organic pollutants are available for oxygen consuming
bacteria.[36, 39]
Figure 6.2: The biodegradability of a distillery wastewater as a function of the ozone dose. Conditions
during the measurements: 20°C, pH = 5.4, 30 l/h gas ﬂow rate, domestic sewage to vinasse volume
ratio = 10. [36]
As illustrated in Figure 6.2 there is an optimal ozone dose. Low doses of ozone are not
suﬃcient to convert all organics and to make the organic matter more susceptible to
microorganisms. Too high ozone concentrations lead to the removal of biodegradable
compounds formed during the oxidation process.[36]
Another eﬀect of preozonation, which improves the subsequent biological treatment, is
the improvement of the sedimentation rate through diﬀerent mechanisms.[36]
Ozone Addition in the Tertiary Treatment
Ozone has anti-bacterial properties and it inactivates viruses. Moreover, it acts as a
biocide and can help in removing algae. This means that ozone can be a good alter-
11
==PAGE:24==
6.4. OZONE’S PATHOGEN KILLING MECHANISMS
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
native to chlorine in the disinfection step of the tertiary treatment.[32, 11] Chlorine
has a high toxicity and can give many storage and handling problems. However, it
is eﬀective as a microbiocide and has a reasonable cost, which is the primary reason
for its wide use in wastewater treatment.[11] When chlorine reacts with organic mat-
ter it can lead to the formation of chlorinated organic disinfection by-products like
trihalomethanes (THMs).[38] If the level of these by-products are not controlled they
may become hazardous to human health. There is some controversy about the actual
risk posed by THMs. Each national health agency uses its own risk rating and safety
factor to set the maximum allowable THM level. In some countries the THM limit is
just set to what is readily achievable instead of basing the limit on an estimate of the
potential risk.[40] When ozone reacts with organic material it also forms disinfection
by-products such as aldehydes and ketones. However, these compounds can easily be
degraded in a bio-ﬁlter and do not pose any risk violating the wastewater or drinking
water standards. Several studies have also proven that ozone, unlike chlorine prod-
ucts, can deactivate resistant micro-organisms.[38] Generally, ozone is eﬀective agains
a much wider spectrum of microorganisms than other disinfectants.[32]
6.4
Ozone’s Pathogen Killing Mechanisms
Now the anti-bacterial and anti-viral properties of ozone will be further investigated.
In this section the pathogen killing mechanisms of ozone is reviewed.
Killing of Bacteria Cells
In the disinfection step of the wastewater process ozone will be able to eliminate various
kinds of bacteria. Bacteria are small single-cell creatures with a primitive structure,
which multiply by division.[41] The cytoplasm of bacteria is encapsulated by a cell
membrane and a rigid cell wall. The cell wall ensures that the bacteria maintain their
shape and protects the cells. The gram-positive bacteria have a thick cell wall consisting
of many layers of peptidoglycans. Gram-negative bacteria have an outer much thinner
membrane which contains lipoprotein and lipopolysaccharides. Aditionally, it contains
a few layers of peptidoglycans beneath this outer membrane.[42, 43] The vital processes
of the bacteria are controlled by a complex enzymatic system.
When ozone comes into contact with the cell wall it begins to oxidise the components
of the cell wall (oxidative burst). This creates a tiny hole in the cell wall and produces
intracellular leakage.[44, 45, 41] Once ozone has entered the cell it will start to oxidise
enzymes, proteins, DNA, and RNA. Thus, ozone does interfere with the metabolism
of bacterium cells by inhibiting and blocking the operation of the enzymatic control
system.[29] As more ozone begins to rupture the cell wall the bacterium loses its shape.
After thousands of ozone collisions within a few seconds the bacterium will die because
of the complete destruction of its wall.[45] The whole process whereby ozone destroys
the cell wall is illustrated in Figure 6.3. Ozone inhibits growth and kills both gram
negative and gram positive bacteria.
Ozone has more destructive eﬀects on gram
positive bacteria compared to gram negative bacteria. This indicates that ozone reacts
more readily with proteins than lipids.[46]
12
==PAGE:25==
6.4. OZONE’S PATHOGEN KILLING MECHANISMS
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
A list of how many diﬀerent types of bacteria that are susceptible to ozone is shown in
Appendix B.
Figure 6.3: The process whereby the cell wall of the bacterium is destroyed by ozone. 1) A healthy
bacterium cell, 2) ozone comes into contact with the cell wall, 3) Oxidation reactions occur, 4) A tiny
hole in the cell wall is created, 5) Lots of tiny holes are created within seconds, 6) the cell loses its
shape and dies.[29]
Killing of Viruses
Viruses penetrate and exploit living cells (host cells). They can only multilpy in host
cells. Outside of these the virus particles exist as lifeless organisms without their own
metabolism. A virus consists of a genome i.e. a nucleic acid-containing core (DNA or
RNA). This core is surrounded by a protein membrane (capsid), which is made up of
protein elements.[47] Ozone kills viruses by diﬀusing through the protein coat into the
nucleaic acid core. Here it attacks and damages viral RNA. At high ozone concentra-
tions, ozone can also destroy the exterior protein shell of the virus.[41]
A list of how many diﬀerent types of viruses that are susceptible to ozone is shown in
Appendix C.
Despite ozone’s great potential to be used in wastewater treatments for anti-bacterial
and anti-viral purposes and for the decomposition of organic matter this technology is
not yet economically attractive. First of all the eﬃciency of ozone is limited because of
its relatively low solubility and fast decomposition rate in water, which is much faster
than in the gas phase.[6, 48] The half-life of ozone in water at 298K and pH = 7 is
approx. 15min.[49] These diﬃculties lead to higher installation and running costs of
the disinfection process than the chlorine systems do.[11] Second of all using ozone
as a wastewater disinfection agent requires high power energy consumption, vacuum
operation, and high voltage.[5] There needs to be developed new techniques for pro-
longing the reactivity and stability of ozone in water before it can be used extensively
in large-scale wastewater treatments.
Micro- and nano-bubbles represent an innovative technology for improving the eﬃ-
ciency of ozone in wastewater treatments. The small bubbles provide higher surface
areas of ozone resulting in a more eﬃcient mass transfer approach between gas and
liquid phase.[49] Several scientists and researchers believe that the bubbles can signif-
icantly improve gas concentration and provide a continuous gas supply.[6] In the next
section the technology of micro- and nano-bubbles will be introduced.
13
==PAGE:26==
6.5. MICRO- AND NANO-BUBBLES
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
6.5
Micro- and Nano-Bubbles
Bubbles are gas-ﬁlled cavities within liquids. When a bubble is formed an interface is
created. The interface possesses diﬀerent properties to the bulk solution. The energy
required to form the interface of the bubble per unit surface area is given by the surface
tension (σ).[50]
No clear, distinct deﬁnition about the sizes of micro-bubbles and nano-bubbles has been
established. Some literature state that micro- and nano-bubbles are tiny bubbles with
diameters of 10-50µm and < 200nm, respectively.[51, 48] Other literary sources state
that micro-bubbles are bubbles of size less than 100µm and nano-bubbles are bubbles
of size less than 1µm.[52, 53] When micro-bubbles are generated in water they give the
water a cloudy or milky appearance. On the other hand a dispersion containing nano-
bubbles will be more transparent because the bubble size is so tiny.[49] The interest for
micro- and nano-bubbles has increased within many industrial ﬁelds due to the fact that
their properties are so diﬀerent from those of mill-scaled bubbles.[54] In the following
subsections some of the important properties of micro- and nano-bubbles regarding
mass transfer and their potential use in wastewater treatments will be reviewed.
6.5.1
Hydrodynamic Properties of Bubbles
The buoyancy of ordinary (mill-scaled) bubbles will cause them to rise and burst on
the liquid surface of aqueous solutions. Therefore, large bubbles are ineﬃcient in gas
transfer.[55] When a bubble rises to the surface and bursts then the change in the
Gibbs energy (∆GT,P,n) is given by[50]:
∆GT,P,n = σ · ∆A
(6.1)
Here ∆A: the change in the interfacial area [m2].
The motion of micro- and nano-bubbles in water is similar to that of hard colloid
particles. The rising velocity of these small bubbles are determined by a balance of the
buoyancy force and the drag forces acting on the bubbles.[48] Micro- and nano-bubbles
have a low buoyancy and hence a very slow rise velocity. For small spherical bubbles
(Re < 0.1) the terminal rise velocity can be calculated using Stoke’s Law [56]:
ub = ρl · g · d2
b
18 · µl
(6.2)
Here ub: terminal rise velocity [m/s], ρl: liquid density [kg/m3], g: gravitational accel-
eration [m/s2], db: bubble diameter [m], and µl: liquid viscosity [Pa · s].
Stoke’s Law (equation (6.2)) can only be applied in case of small bubbles with immobile
surfaces. If the bubble instead has a mobile surface, then the Hadamard-Rybczynski
equation can be used to determine the terminal rise velocity[56]:
ub = ρl · g · d2
b
12µl
(6.3)
14
==PAGE:27==
6.5. MICRO- AND NANO-BUBBLES
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
Figure 6.4: The bubble rising velocity against the bubble radius.
The arrows on top of the plot
indicate in which geometrical shape the bubbles exit the water.[56]
Using the Hadamard-Rybczynski equation (Equation (6.3)) the terminal velocity be-
comes 1.5 times higher than the Stoke’s velocity.[52]
If contaminants like surfactants, proteins, or detergents are present this will make the
bubble surface immobile and result in a slower rise velocity. With large bubbles, the
surface moves more freely, and is only little aﬀected by the presence of surface active
agents.[57] On the other hand the surface of small bubbles of size less than 50µm in
diameter are often quickly contaminated by surface impurities usually present in all
water.[6, 57, 48] The terminal rise velocity of a micro-bubble of radius 10µm is 50µm/s,
which is equal to 3 mm/min. The slow rise velocity leads to a larger residence time.[58]
Figure 6.4 shows a plot of the rising velocity against the bubble radius. Moreover, it
shows that larger bubbles typically exit the solutions in the shape of ellipsoids or spher-
ical caps, whereas smaller bubbles have a more spherical shape in solution.
Micro-bubbles can remain stable in water in the order of several minutes, leading to
a higher gas holdup.[56] However, they will gradually decrease in size and collapse.
This is because of the dissolution of interior gases into the surrounding water.[49, 51]
The relatively rapid shrinking of the micro-bubble leads to a signiﬁcant increase in
pressure.
When the rate of the pressure increase is suﬃciently high, the tempera-
ture within the microbubble also rises because of adiabatic compression. So when the
micro-bubble collapses an area, with a pressure of a few thousand atmospheres and
a temperature of a few thousand degrees, will be created. Even though this extreme
reaction site represents a very small area it has enough potential to force the decom-
position of gas molecules in micro-bubbles. Due to the pyrolytic decomposition within
the collapsing bubbles, ·OH radicals and shock waves can be generated at the gas-liquid
interface.[51, 48] ·OH radicals have a high standard potential of 2.80V. So the presence
15
==PAGE:28==
6.5. MICRO- AND NANO-BUBBLES
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
of ·OH radicals is advantageous because it can also contribute to the degradation of
wastewater, especially in anti-bacterial and anti-smell processes.[11] Furthermore, col-
lapsing micro-bubbles can generate nano-bubbles.[59]
Nano-bubbles remain stable in water for months and do not burst. Figure 6.5 sum-
marises the behaviour of bubbles of various sizes.
Figure 6.5: Schematic drawing showing mill-scaled bubbles (ordinary bubble), micro-, and nano-
bubbles.[51]
It is still uncertain why the micro- and nano-bubbles remain stable for signiﬁcantly
longer times than mill-scaled bubbles.[54] As will be described in the next subsec-
tion the long life-time and stability of nano-bubbles should be thermodynamically
impossible.[50]
6.5.2
Internal Bubble Pressure
Bubbles are unstable with respect to their size. In order to maintain a constant size the
gas within the bubble must be at equilibrium with the dissolved gas in solution. Most
often this condition is not met or is rather impossible to maintain. A small perturbation
will disrupt the equilibrium causing the bubble to either grow very large very quickly
and to be removed from the solution through buoyancy or to shrink from existence.
This is known as the Laplace Pressure Bubble Catastrophe (LPBC). The LPBC has a
major impact on nano-bubble research and indicates that the tiny bubbles cannot be
thermodynamically stable.[50] The reason why equilibrium can never be maintained is
the Laplace pressure. The Laplace pressure is the pressure diﬀerence across a curved
surface forming the interface between two static ﬂuids such as water and air.[60] The
Young-Laplace equation expresses the relationship between the pressure and the bubble
diameter:
Pg −Pl = 4σ
db
(6.4)
Here P: the gas pressure [Pa], Pl: liquid pressure [Pa], σ: surface tension [J/m2], and
db: bubble diameter [m].
16
==PAGE:29==
6.5. MICRO- AND NANO-BUBBLES
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
The gas solubility increases with pressure. This means that when the Laplace pressure
increases so does the gas solubility in solution. So the smaller the bubble the greater
the solubility of gas. A small perturbation resulting in the bubble becoming marginally
larger will reduce the Laplace pressure. In turn this will reduce the solubility of the
gas in the solution surrounding the bubble. So suddenly more gas will diﬀuse into
the bubble causing it to grow. This further lowers the Laplace pressure and the gas
solubility. Thus, a positive feedback loop has started where the bubble continues to
grow and can never obtain equilibrium. A similar feedback loop causing the bubble
to shrink is created if a small perturbation causes the bubble to become marginally
smaller.[50]
As seen in equation (6.4) the internal bubble pressure is inversely proportional to the
bubble size. Based on equation (6.4) it is expected that nano-bubbles have an extremely
high internal pressure.[61] It can be calculated that a nano-bubble of 20nm in diameter
has an internal excess pressure of 14.5MPa. The high Laplace pressure inside the nano-
bubbles should make them collapse and rapidly dissolve into solution. Theoretically,
the 20nm nano-bubble should only have a lifetime of ≈1µs. So the Young-Laplace
equation implies that nano-bubbles are thermodynamically never stable. Some scien-
tists question the applicability of the Young-Laplace equation for nano-bubbles. Other
studies carried out by Matsumoto and Tanaka (2008) has validated the applicability
of this equation even in nano-scale.[61, 48]
6.5.3
Bubble Stability
This subsection brieﬂy presents diﬀerent theories describing why the nano-bubbles
might be stable despite the theoretical predictions of the Laplace pressure. There are
four diﬀerent explanations to why the nano-bubbles might be stable:
Explanation 1
The contaminants on the nano-bubbles lower their surface tension and hence the
Laplace pressure thereby stabilizing the bubbles.[50]
Explanation 2
Generally, micro-bubbles and nano-bubbles have negative zeta potentials in neutral
solutions. The zeta potential of micro-bubbles in distilled water is -35mV.[48] The
zeta potential of nano-bubbles in water is around -32mV at pH 7.28.[62] It is believed
that the high absolute zeta potential prevents nano-bubbles from coalescence, improves
their stability, and slows their rise velocity. The negativly charged bubbles should be
able to absorb hydrogen ions on their surface, thereby making Coulomb repulsion forces
compensate surface tension forces.[61, 62]
Explanation 3
The negative zeta potential of the bubbles might also aﬀect the hydrogen-bonds in the
17
==PAGE:30==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
water. This means that the hydrogen-bond network at the gas-liquid interface has a
diﬀerent structure from that of the bulk water. According to Ohgaki et al., the water
molecules around the nano-bubbles may form shells of hard hydrogen-bonded icelike
structures.[61] At the same time the presence of nano-bubbles weaken the hydrogen
bond network in the bulk water.[62] The hard hydrogen-structure near the interface
results in a reduction of the diﬀusivity of gases through the interfacial ﬁlm.[51]
Explanation 4
The eﬀects of having high concentrations of nano-bubbles have also been considered.
Nano-bubbles protect each other from diﬀusion and hence from dissolving by a shield-
ing eﬀect.
The diﬀusive outﬂux from a bubble is suppressed by a locally eﬀective
increase in dissolved gas in solution due to surrounding bubbles. For this shielding
eﬀect to happen the spacing between the bubbles in a cluster must not be too large.
[63, 50]
For now there is no accepted explanation for the long-term stability of nano-bubbles.
And it has not yet been validated that the nano-bubbles are stable. This report will
not be concerned with the stability of the nano-bubbles. So this section was just to
give a greater insight into some of the challenges and doubts related to nano-bubbles.
After this brief introduction to micro- and nano-bubbles one better understands the
future possibilities as well as the challenges. One also gets a feeling of just how new a
research topic it is. Due to their larger surface areas and higher internal pressures the
micro- and nano-bubbles present rapid mass transfer rates and increased gas solubil-
ity. They remain for a relatively long time in water and transport with the water-ﬂow
thereby providing a continuous gas supply. Furthermore, micro-bubbles are able to
generate strong oxidizing agents like ·OH radicals when they collapse. All of the above
is highly beneﬁcial in wastewater treatments and especially in ozonation processes.
Micro- and nano-bubbles ﬁlled with ozone have the potential to change the wastewater
treatment processes fundamentally.
6.6
Mass Transfer
In order to understand the mechanisms involved in the use of ozone micro- and nano-
bubbles for wastewater treatment, it is essential to understand the principles governing
the mass transfer of a gas into a liquid phase. The following subsections will describe
the general theory of diﬀusion, various mass transfer theories, and the deﬁnitions of
mass transfer coeﬃcients.
6.6.1
Theory of Diﬀusion
The most fundamental concept to understand is the concept of diﬀusion. Through
diﬀusion matter will always move from high concentration to low concentration. This
18
==PAGE:31==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
movement of matter results from Brownian motions and a driving force from the chem-
ical potential.[64] This can be described mathematically by the one-dimensional form
of Fick’s law of diﬀusion[11]:
J = −D · dc
dx
(6.5)
This equation indicates that the mass ﬂux per unit area (J) is proportional to the con-
centration gradient
  dc
dx

. The proportionality constant (D) is the diﬀusion coeﬃcient.[65]
The principle of diﬀusion is easily illustrated in Figure 6.6. This ﬁgure shows the grad-
ual development of the concentration proﬁle across a thin ﬁlm in response to a sudden
step change on the left side of the ﬁlm. The ﬁgure presents four stages: a) Equilib-
rium is obtained and the concentration is equal in the entire medium. b) A sudden
step change occurs where the concentration of the left side is increased. c) The con-
centration proﬁle builds up. Here the material diﬀuses from the left hand side (high
concentration) to the right hand side (low concentration). d) Steady state is obtained.
Here the ﬂux of material is constant and the transport of material across the medium
is constant.[65, 66]
Figure 6.6: The concentration proﬁle across a thin ﬁlm following a sudden step change on the left
hand side. [66]
Usually, diﬀusion is a very slow process and becomes the rate limiting step in many
industrial processes when it occurs simultaneously with other chemical mechanisms
and reactions. The diﬀusion strongly depends on the physical state of the medium.
The diﬀusion coeﬃcient in diﬀerent phases vary as follows[65, 67]:
Gas-phase:
around 0.1
cm2/sec [67]
Liquid-phase:
around 10−5
cm2/sec [67]
Solid-phase:
around 10−30
cm2/sec [67]
Polymers and glasses:
around 10−8
cm2/sec [67]
From the values in the box above it is clear to see that the diﬀusion in solids is extremely
slow compared to the diﬀusion in liquids and in the gaseous phase. Moreover, the
diﬀusion in gases is much faster than in liquids. The diﬀusion coeﬃcients for gases are
19
==PAGE:32==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
inversely proportional to pressure, so doubling the pressure should cut the diﬀusion
in half. The gas coeﬃcients vary by 1.5 to 1.8 to the power of the temperature. For
liquids the diﬀusion coeﬃcient depends on the viscosity of the liquid. [67, 68]
6.6.2
Mass Transfer Across a Phase Boundary
In an ozonation process the gas inside the micro- or nano-bubble must dissolve in the
surrounding liquid before it is able to react with the inorganic or organic contaminants.[48]
This means that mass transfer must occur from the gas phase to the liquid phase. Gen-
erally, the mass transfer rate between two diﬀerent ﬂuids will depend on the physical
properties of the two phases, the concentration diﬀerence, the interfacial area, and the
degree of turbulence.[69] The process of the gas exiting the bubble and entering the
bulk liquid happens in three stages [11]:
Stage 1:
First of all ozone must be transported in the gas stream to the gas-liquid-interface by
molecular diﬀusion.
Stage 2:
Then ozone must be transferred across the gas-liquid boundary. This part of the trans-
port process can be complicated by various resistances on the liquid or the gas side of
the boundary.
Stage 3:
Finally, ozone must move from the liquid side of the interface into the bulk of the liquid.
The above description of the ozone molecules’ path from the interior of the gas bubble
to the surrounding liquid is much simpliﬁed. In reality the mass transfer behaviour
can be complicated by many chemical reactions.[11] However, this simpliﬁed descrip-
tion highlights one fundamental element in the mass transfer. When mass transfer is
occurring from one physical phase to another then there is a boundary, which must be
crossed. Many models have been developed to describe the conditions in the region
of such a boundary and its eﬀect on the overall mass transfer system. Some of these
models are [11, 69]:
• Two-ﬁlm theory
• Penetration theory
• Surface Renewal theory
Below each of these theories will be brieﬂy described.
Two-Film Theory
The Two-Film theory was developed by Lewis Whitman in 1923. This was the ﬁrst
real attempt to describe the conditions of transfer of material from one ﬂuid stream
20
==PAGE:33==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
to another. It is the simplest and the oldest method describing mass transfer across a
phase boundary. Figure 6.7 is a schematic representation of the two-ﬁlm theory.
Figure 6.7: Schematic drawing showing the principles of mass transfer according to the two-ﬁlm
theory. The Figure is modiﬁed from [48] and [70].
The two-ﬁlm theory assumes that when the two ﬂuid phases are brought in contact with
each other then a sharp phase boundary is created. In reality no sharp demarcation
will exist. According to the theory a thin layer of laminar ﬁlm exists on each side of this
phase boundary as depicted in Figure 6.7.[71] The theory suggests that the resistance
to mass transfer in each physical phase can be regarded as located in these two thin
ﬁlms close to the gas-liquid-interface. Therefore, it is within these two ﬁlm layers that
the concentration starts to decrease. Mass transfer by convection within these ﬁlms
is assumed to be insigniﬁcant, so the transport only happens through steady state
diﬀusion.[72] The two-ﬁlm theory assumes that the concentration gradients in these
layers are linear. In reality this is not the case. In the bulk of the gas phase and the
liquid phase, the natural random motion of the particles is enhanced or supplemented
by turbulent eddies (circulating currents). This turbulence is suﬃcient for the concen-
tration gradients to become zero.[73] It is assumed that the resistance to mass transfer
across the interface is negligible, so the concentrations on each side of the interface are
related by a phase-equilibrium relationship (e.g. Henry’s law). The quantity of the
jump in concentration at the interface is determined by the speciﬁc phase-equilibrium
relationship.[11]
The two-ﬁlm model is limited by the fact that it does not reproduce the conditions in
most practical mass transfer equipment. The theory assumes that the mass transfer
is a steady-state process. So the theory is only applicable if the time taken for the
concentration gradients to become established is very small compared with the time
of transfer or if the capacities of the ﬁlms are negligible. Furthermore, it can be quite
21
==PAGE:34==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
diﬃcult to determine the thickness of the two ﬁlm layers on either side of the interface.
Despite this the theory is still extensively used to describe mass transfer in some
cases.[69].
Penetration Theory
The penetration theory was developed in 1935 by Higbie. In many cases the contact
time between two ﬂuids are too short to achieve steady-state. The penetration theory
is considered as an improvement of the two-ﬁlm theory in that sense that it assumes
an unsteady state mass transfer process.
The theory is very complex so in order to describe it as easily as possible it will be
explained by describing a bubble rising in water. As the bubble rises liquid elements
become attached to the surface of the bubble, thereby creating the gas-liquid-interface
as seen in Phase 1 in Figure 6.8. In this ﬁgure the liquid elements are represented by
the blue squares.
Figure 6.8: Illustration of the principles of the penetration theory. The blue squares represent liquid
elements. The red square represents a new liquid element. In Phase 1 the liquid elements are attached
to the surface of the bubble. Then in Phase 2 one liquid element is replaced by a new element (the
red square).
Each liquid element is in direct contact with the gas phase (the bubble) for a ﬁxed
amount of time.
The residence time at the gas-liquid interface is the same for all
elements. Unsteady state mass transfer occurs from the bubble to the attached liquid
elements, as long as the elements stay on the bubble surface.
At the interface an
equilibrium is established following e.g.
Henry’s Law.
After some time the liquid
elements detach from the bubble.[74] When the elements detach they are replaced by
new liquid elements brought to the interface by eddies from within the solvent. The
eddies also carry the old liquid elements back into the bulk of the liquid. The process is
illustrated in Figure 6.8, where the red water element replaces one of the blue elements
from Phase 1 to Phase 2. This process replacing elements at the interface repeats for
some time. If t →∞then near 100% saturation in the whole liquid phase should be
achieved due to the constant mixing of ’old liquid elements’ with the bulk.
The problem with the penetration theory is that it assumes that the depth of the liquid
22
==PAGE:35==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
phase is inﬁnite. This assumption is only justiﬁable if the time the liquid elements are
exposed to the gas is relatively short.
So the shorter the exposure time the more
accurate is the penetration model.[11, 69]
Surface Renewal Theory
The surface renewal theory was developed by Danckwerts and presented in 1951. It is
an extension of the penetration theory. In respect to two assumptions it diﬀers from
the penetration theory. Firstly, this theory pictures the liquid phase as being divided
into two regions: 1) a large well-mixed region, and 2) an interfacial region, which is
renewed so rapidly that it begins to resemble a thick ﬁlm.[74] This is illustrated in
Figure 6.9.
Figure 6.9: Schematic drawing of the principles of the surface renewal theory.[74]
Secondly, there is a diﬀerent perception of the ﬂuid contact times in the surface renewal
theory compared to the penetration theory. In the penetration theory Higbie presup-
pose that the contact time between the two ﬂuid phases is constant and the same at
all positions. However, in the surface renewal theory it is suggested that the liquid
elements brought to the interface will remain there for varying periods of time. The
contact time between the gas phase and the individual liquid elements follows a distri-
bution function (the distribution of ages). All liquid elements at the gas-liquid-interface
have the same probability of being dislodged from the contact area and replaced by
another ﬂuid element. This is the reason for calling the theory the ’surface renewal
theory’. The time for surface renewal is as uncertain as the contact times in the pen-
etration theory. Therefore both theories are not applicable for the calculation of the
quantities involved in mass transfer.[75] The main disadvantage of the model following
this theory is that all the velocity ﬂuctuation eﬀects are combined in a single-term
surface renewal rate, which is diﬃcult to measure.[76]
23
==PAGE:36==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
6.6.3
Mass Transfer Coeﬃcients
Some essential parameters used in the design of mass-transfer theories are the mass
transfer coeﬃcients. They are deﬁned in such a way that:
Diﬀusive ﬂux = Mass transfer coeﬃcient × Concentration diﬀerence
(6.6)
This means that the local mass transfer coeﬃcient in a liquid (kl) is given by:
kl =
JA|s
cA0 −cAl
(6.7)
Here kl: the local liquid mass transfer coeﬃcient [m/s], cA0: the concentration of A
at the surface of the ﬂuid [mol/m3], cAl: the concentration in the bulk ﬂuid [mol/m3],
and JA|s: the diﬀusive ﬂux of A on the ﬂuid surface [mol/(m2 · s)].
As seen in Equation (6.7) the diﬀerence in concentrations between the surface and the
bulk of ﬂuid is the driving force for the mass transport.
In a gas phase the local mass transfer coeﬃcient (kg) is mostly deﬁned using the partial
pressure:
kg =
JA|s
pA0 −pAg
(6.8)
Here kg: the local gas mass transfer coeﬃcient [m/s], pA0: the partial pressure of A at
the surface of the ﬂuid [Pa], and pAg: the partial pressure of A in the bulk ﬂuid [Pa].
In Equation (6.8) the driving force is the diﬀerence in the partial pressures.[77]
As seen by Equation (6.7) and (6.8) the mass transfer coeﬃcients are diﬀusion rate
constants, which relates the mass transfer rate, the mass transfer area, and the change
in concentration/partial pressure.[78] The mass transfer coeﬃcients so deﬁned should
account for the physical properties of the system such as the diﬀusivity, the ﬂuid
density, the viscosity, and the ﬂuid motion.[77]
The mass transfer coeﬃcients can be applied to quantify the mass transfer between
diﬀerent physical phases or between ﬂuid mixtures.[78] The ozone contained in micro-
and nano-bubbles has to travel through two diﬀerent phases - a gas phase and a liquid
phase. So the mass transfer through both of these phases have to be described. The
overall mass transfer coeﬃcients in the liquid and gas phases, Kl and Kg, are given by
[48]:
1
Kl
=
1
KgHcc
= 1
kl
+
1
kgHcc
(6.9)
Where Kl: the overall mass transfer coeﬃcient in the liquid phase [m/s], Kg: the over-
all mass transfer coeﬃcient in the gas phase [m/s], and Hcc: Henry’s constant [-].
The whole derivation of the expression in Equation (6.9) can be seen in greater detail
in this reference: [77]. Equation (6.9) shows that the overall mass transfer coeﬃcients
24
==PAGE:37==
6.6. MASS TRANSFER
CHAPTER 6. LITERATURE SURVEY
depend on the average local mass transport coeﬃcients (kl and kg), and on the slope
of the equilibrium curve (Hcc).[77]
In the case of micro- and nano-bubbles the gas molecules must only diﬀuse through a
small distance. Furthermore, according to Johnson and Davis the mass transfer resis-
tance for O3 mainly lies in the liquid phase because of the relatively low solubility of
O3 in water. This entails that the gas-phase mass transfer resistance,
1
kgH , is negligible,
which results in that Kl ≈kl.[79] In other words the mass transfer for sparingly soluble
gases are liquid-ﬁlm controlled.
In order to be able to determine the mass transfer of the micro- and nano-bubbles one
needs an expression relating the mass transfer coeﬃcient, kl, to other parameters in
the system. Mass transfer coeﬃcients can be estimated from many diﬀerent correla-
tions and theoretical equations, which are functions of material properties and the ﬂow
regime. The mass transfer coeﬃcient can e.g. be estimated by relations between dif-
ferent dimensionless groups, such as the Sherwood number (Sh), the Reynolds number
(Re), and the Schmidt number (Sc).[77] An explanation of these dimensionless groups
and their interdependence can be seen in Appendix D.
Commonly, the mass-transfer coeﬃcient of dissolving bubbles depend upon the inter-
face mobility. Below the relationship between kl, Sh, Re, and Sc will be described for
bubbles with both mobile and immobile surfaces.[57]
Mobile Surface
If the interface is mobile then the contact time with the liquid is short. In this case
Higbie’s theory is applicable [57]:
Sh = 1.13 · Re

Here db: the diameter of the bubble [m], ul: the velocity of the ﬂuid [m/s], ρl: the

The mass transfer coeﬃcients calculated using the two equations (6.11) and (6.13)
diﬀer by a factor of ﬁve. So before applying either of the equations it is essential to
know when the bubble behaves like a solid or has a mobile surface.[80] Figure 6.10 is
a plot of the liquid mass transfer coeﬃcient as a function of the bubble diameter. The
circles, triangles, and squares are experimental data obtained from diﬀerent sources.
The lines represent the theoretical results obtained using the Fr¨ossling theory and the
Higbie theory, respectively.
Figure 6.10: A plot showing the correlation between the mass transfer theories and experimental
results. Both the theoretical predictions of Fr¨ossling and Higbie are shown.[80]
Figure 6.10 shows that the experimental mass transfer coeﬃcients follow the Fr¨ossling
theory closely at small bubble diameters. As the bubble diameters increase, the Kl
values also increase.
This indicates that the bubble begins to have a more mobile
surface. When the bubble has a diameter above 200µm, the experimental Kl values
agree well with Higbie’s theory. As mentioned in section 6.5.1 smaller bubbles tend to
have immobilzed surfaces due to contamination at their surfaces. So the mass transfer
coeﬃcients of small bubbles are reduced because of these physical barriers.[81] Unfortu-
nately, there is little concrete data in the literature regarding mass transfer coeﬃcients
for bubbles in the micro- and nano-scale.
All the mass transfer theories relate the mass transfer ﬂux to a concentration diﬀerence
driving force, and a liquid-phase mass transfer coeﬃcient.[73] So several mathematical
expressions for mass transfer coeﬃcients are established based on the various mass
transfer theories. An overview of some of these expressions can be seen in Appendix
E.
According to the mass transfer theory the gas inside the micro- or nano-bubble will
continue to dissolve in the liquid phase (until a certain level of saturation is reached),
thereby increasing the ozone content in the liquid. The rate of increase in the ozone

concentration in the liquid phase (the volumetric gas transfer rate) is given by:
dc

(6.14)
Here a: the interfacial area per unit volume [m2/m3], and cS is the saturation concen-
tration of the gas in water [mol/m3].
The area of the interface is rarely known. So in equation (6.14) the product of kl and
the interfacial area per unit volume is used instead. This product is known as the
volumetric mass transfer coeﬃcient. a can be calculated in the following way:
a = 6εg

(6.15)
Here εg: the gas hold-up, and d32: the Sauter mean diameter [m].
Many researchers such as Kaster et al. (1990), Ago et al. (2005), and Bredwell and
Worden (1998) have observed a signiﬁcant increase in the volumetric mass trans-
fer coeﬃcients of gases by applying micro-bubbles compared to conventional bubble
techniques.[48]
Now some general knowledge regarding the advantages and challenges of using ozone
in wastewater treatments has been obtained. This has made it possible to view the
project in a much broader perspective. Furthermore, the theoretical background on
mass transfer has provided essential tools for the development of mathematical models
describing the mass transfer of ozone using micro- and nano-bubbles. The mathemat-
ical models are developed in the next chapter. The method used in the subsequent
model development is to start with a simple model and slowly add to the complexity
of the ﬁrst model.

==PAGE:40==
Mathematical Model Development
In this chapter ﬁve diﬀerent models will be established step by step through math-
ematical derivations.
Afterwards computational simulations will be carried out by
implementing the models in MatLab.
Figure 7.1: An illustration of the system through which the gas diﬀusion occurs.
The purpose of all ﬁve models is to describe the diﬀusion of ozone from a bubble and
into the bulk liquid. A schematic drawing of the system to be modelled is seen in Fig-
ure 7.1. The area bounded by R1 will be referred to as ’the bulk gas phase’, whereas
the area bounded by R2 - which includes both the bulk gas and the gas ﬁlm - is re-
ferred to as ’the bubble’. These deﬁnitions are extremely important to remember in
the development of the mathematical models. Ozone is initially stored in the bulk gas
phase of the bubble. Then it is transported from the bulk gas phase through the gas
ﬁlm and the liquid ﬁlm (the zone between R1 and R3) and into the bulk liquid. The
main focus is on the ozone transport through the two ﬁlm layers.
The mathematical models are only designed to describe the diﬀusion from a single bub-
ble, even though multiple bubbles will be applied in the wastewater treatment process.
In reality, the bubble will move around in the bulk liquid. However, the movement of
28
==PAGE:41==
7.1. THE FIRST MODEL
CHAPTER 7. MATHEMATICAL MODEL DEVELOPMENT
the bubble is also neglected in all the models and the bubble is considered as stagnant.
It is possible to deﬁne the thickness of the stagnant layers using the radii in Figure 7.1:
The thickness of the gas ﬁlm: δgf = R2 - R1
The thickness of the liquid ﬁlm: δlf = R3 - R2
7.1
The First Model
The ﬁrst model is regarded as the basic model. The more advanced models presented
later in the project are based on the same set-up as this model. Due to the importance
of this ﬁrst model a detailed mathematical description of how the model is established
step by step will follow. But before the actual modelling work can begin, one has to
set up some simplifying assumptions to ease the calculations. So the development of
the ﬁrst mathematical model will be based on the following assumptions:
General Assumptions
• No bubble deformation occurs so the bubble is spherical at all times, causing the
two stagnant layers to have an spherical shape as well.
• The bubble has a constant volume. This means that the outer radii of the bulk
gas phase and the two ﬁlm layers remain the same throughout the whole process.
• The diﬀusivity of the gas does not depend on the ozone concentration. This means
that the diﬀusion coeﬃcient is constant in both the gas ﬁlm and the liquid ﬁlm.
• The bulk gas phase is well-mixed.
• The bulk liquid phase is well-mixed.
• Ozone is rapidly transported away from the outer surface of the liquid ﬁlm to be
mixed with the rest of the bulk liquid.
• A level of saturation in the bulk liquid is not reached.
Generally, the ozone
concentration in the bulk water surrounding the bubble is considered to be very
low.
• The boundaries between the gas and the liquid phases are sharp.
So are the
boundaries between the bulk phases and the ﬁlm layers.
• There is no resistance to mass transfer at the gas-liquid-interface. Thereby the
concentrations on each side of the interface are related to each other by a phase-
equilibrium relationship.
• The solubility of ozone can be described by Henry’s law.
• The amount of ozone in the bulk gas phase is kept constant throughout the entire
diﬀusion process, thereby providing an inﬁnite ozone supply.
29
==PAGE:42==
7.1. THE FIRST MODEL
CHAPTER 7. MATHEMATICAL MODEL DEVELOPMENT
• This single bubble is not aﬀected by the presence of other bubbles in the sur-
rounding medium.
7.1.1
The Mole Balance
The ﬁrst step in the development of all mathematical models describing mass transport
is to set up a mass or a mole balance. In order to do so, control volumes have to be
clearly deﬁned. In this case the control volumes will be two spherical shells of thickness
∆r as seen in Figure 7.2. To the left of the gas-liquid-interface in Figure 7.2 is the gas
ﬁlm, where the compounds are allowed to diﬀuse freely. On the right hand side of the
interface is the liquid ﬁlm, in which the compounds are also assumed to diﬀuse freely.
Figure 7.2: An illustration of the scenario and the control volume used in the mathematical derivations.
The control volumes are the hatched areas.
Each control volume will have a surface area of S, which is given by:
S = 4πr2
(7.1)
The general mole balance equation can be written as follows:
Moles Accumulated = Moles Generated + (Moles in at r) −(Moles out at r + ∆r)
(7.2)
In this case the formation term will not be included because no chemical reaction takes
place in the system.
First of all the mole balance across the liquid ﬁlm (the right control volume) is set
up over a small time interval ∆t. The elements in this unsteady state mole balance is
shown in Table 7.1.

7.1.2
Turning PDE into ODE
Equation (7.6) and (7.7) can be solved analytically in terms of an inﬁnite sum. They are
solved using separation of variables ﬁnding a basis of solutions consisting of products of
exponentials and spherical Bessel functions. One can then use boundary conditions to
select the linear combination of these functions that gives the appropriate solutions.[82]
These calculations will involve quite complicated mathematical expressions. In most
31
==PAGE:44==
7.1. THE FIRST MODEL
CHAPTER 7. MATHEMATICAL MODEL DEVELOPMENT
cases partial diﬀerential equations are diﬃcult to solve analytically so they must be
solved numerically. The PDE’s deﬁned by Equation (7.6) and (7.7) can be solved nu-
merically using computer software by converting the PDE’s into ordinary diﬀerential
equations (ODE’s). In order to carry out this transformation one must apply the ﬁnite
diﬀerence method and discretize the spherical volume into several smaller compart-
ments. The compartments should be equally spaced and each compartment must have
an ODE linked to it. The discretization will result in a system of N coupled ordinary
diﬀerential equations, where N indicates the number of discretized elements. Figure
7.3 is a simple illustration of the discretization scheme.

In the expressions above ϵf is an error function representing the truncation error, which
also accounts for discretization errors. The truncation error resulting from the ﬁnite
mathematical description is related to the concept of numerical diﬀusion. When the
continuous diﬀerential equations are being discretized into ﬁnite diﬀerence equations,
the discrete equations will be more diﬀusive than the original mathematical equations.
The deviation between the two equation systems (original and discrete) depends on
the system being simulated and the type of discretization used. The truncation er-
ror and thereby the numerical diﬀusion can be reduced in two ways: 1) by creating
a more ﬁnely spaced discretization lattice, and 2) making higher order discretization
schemes.[83] So when ∆r →0 then ϵf(∆r) →0.

such a way that it can easily be solved using mathematical programming software. One
way in which this can be done is presented in the next section.

7.1.4
Initial and Boundary Conditions
The system and its solutions are properly deﬁned and shaped by a supporting set of
conditions. So in order to complete the model some initial and boundary conditions
must be established.
Initial Conditions:
All ozone is initially contained within the bulk gas phase. So in the beginning neither
the gas ﬁlm nor the liquid ﬁlm contain any ozone:
c(ri, 0) = 0
for R1 < ri < R2 and t = 0
c(ri, 0) = 0
for R2 < ri < R3 and t = 0

c0 is outside of the discretization scheme and is not a ”legal” concentration. However,
the bulk gas phase is assumed to act as the neighbouring compartment to compart-
ment 1, so that c0 = cg. This means that the 0’th compartment has the same ozone
concentration as inside the bulk gas phase. In this case it is assumed that cg is constant
and does not change as the mass transfer progresses. So compartment 0 acts as an
inﬁnite source of material to supply into compartment 1.

Boundary Condition at the Gas-Liquid-Interface:
The interface needs to be described by two boundary conditions. In order to set up
proper boundary conditions at the interface two diﬀerent approaches were applied.
Each of these approaches and the resulting boundary conditions will be described be-
low:
The First Approach
In this ﬁrst approach a new ’ﬁctive’ compartment, Ni, is added to the discretiza-
tion scheme as seen in Figure 7.4. This compartment should represent the gas-liquid-
interface. Here ’ﬁctive’ means that the compartment is not an actual part of the real
physical system like all the other compartments. It is only added and used to ease the
mathematical calculations.
Figure 7.4: The discretization scheme with the additional compartment Ni.
The concentration in the compartment Ni-1 is expressed by the following diﬀerential
equation:
dcN−1
dt
= Dgf·

cN−2 ·
 1

1


−cN−1 ·
2
∆r2 + cNi ·
 1
∆r2 +
1
rN−1 · ∆r

(7.30)
In Equation (7.30) the concentration cNi is unknown. The general idea is to express
the concentration at Ni, cNi, through a linear extrapolation. Thereby being able to
subsequently calculate the concentration of cNi−1 using Equation ((7.30)). The linear
extrapolation connects the concentrations: cNi−2, cNi−1, and cNi. So in the end it
is possible to express cNi as a linear function of the known concentrations cNi−2 and
cNi−1. The concentrations on either side of the interface in compartment Ni-1 and
Ni+1 are linked through Henry’s law. The mathematical derivations related to this
ﬁrst approach is described in greater detail in Appendix F.
This ﬁrst attempt to describe the diﬀusion process across the interface resulted in a
singular matrix, and hence no solutions could be found.
Perhaps this approach failed because the interface between two compartments should
be represented by two interface compartments instead of one. Furthermore, it is un-
likely that there will be a linear relationship between the concentrations in the three
last compartments in the gas ﬁlm at all times. So this assumption of linearity may
have caused the system of equations to collapse. Instead a second approach was tried.
36
==PAGE:49==
7.1. THE FIRST MODEL
CHAPTER 7. MATHEMATICAL MODEL DEVELOPMENT
This approach had a successful outcome.
The Second Approach
In this approach two ’ﬁctive’ interface compartments are introduced in the discretiza-
tion scheme to ease the calculations as seen in Figure 7.5.
Figure 7.5: Schematic drawing of the discretization scheme. The compartments Ni,gf and Ni,lf are
the two ’ﬁctive’ compartments.
First of all it is assumed that the two parts of the interface are in equilibrium described
by Henry’s law:
cNi,lf = Hcc · cNi,gf
(7.31)
Usually, ozone follows a Henry’s law equilibrium relationship with water, so this is a
valid assumption.[11]. Equation (7.31) is the ﬁrst boundary condition.
The second boundary condition is a criterion of equal ﬂux across the interface. The
ﬂux between compartment Ni-1 and compartment Ni,gf is given by:
Jgf|R2 = Dgf · cNi,gf −cNi−1
∆r
(7.32)
The ﬂux between compartment Ni,lf and compartment Ni+1 is given by:
Jlf|R2 = Dlf · cNi+1 −cNi,lf
∆r
(7.33)
The two ﬂuxes are set equal to one another. This leads to:
Dgf · cNi,gf −cNi−1
∆r
= Dlf · cNi+1 −cNi,lf
∆r
