When XO3,initial is small, the driving force decreases due to several factors: 1) the large
percentage of linear proﬁles relative to non-linear proﬁles, 2) the dilution of ozone by
the inert gases and 3) the larger average bubble size. Generally, the diﬀusive ﬂux is in-
versely proportional to the radius so the shrinkage steepens the concentration gradient
and increases the ﬂux. This is the main reason for the fast reactions at high XO3,initial
values.[89]
Comparison with Literature
The above system behaviour is also described in the research article by R. Worden and
M. Bredwell (reference [89]). They have developed mathematical models for describing
diﬀusion of oxygen from a shrinking micro-bubble in a pool of stagnant liquid. Their
model procedure is reminiscent of the one used in this project. They start by estab-
lishing an unsteady state mass balance. Then they perform a spatial discretization
based on a central diﬀerence method. They have also come to the conclusion that
using micro-bubbles with a high fraction of transferable gas increases the mass transfer
rate, thereby decreasing the lifetime of the bubble. They found that the mass transfer
rate of a micro-bubble containing pure oxygen is about 14 times greater than for an air
86
==PAGE:99==
8.3. MODEL 3
CHAPTER 8. MODEL ANALYSIS
bubble (containing only 21% oxygen). Figure 8.36 shows the results of R. Worden and
M. Bredwell’s research. In this ﬁgure it is clear to see that the average mass transfer
rate increases, while the time to reach 80% mass transfer decreases.[89]
Figure 8.36: A graphical representation of the results of R. Worden and M. Bredwell. The eﬀects of
the initial oxygen mole fraction in a micro-bubble on the average mass transfer rate and the time for
80% mass transfer.[89]
Choice of Bubble Lifetime in the Wastewater Treatment
In designing an eﬃcient wastewater treatment it is important to produce only the
amount of ozone necessary. There is no point in ﬁlling the wastewater with too much
ozone so that as it will not be consumed but instead decomposes. This will only be a
waste of money generating ozone to no avail.
Ozone can be used in the wastewater process as a reagent for two types of general
processes: 1) reactions that are so fast that they are only limited by the slow mass
transfer of ozone into the liquid phase, and 2) the reactions that are limited due to
the reaction kinetics of the material to be ionized. The reaction rate between bacteria
and ozone is very rapid. So if the purpose is to disinfect the water, the mass transfer
of ozone should be as rapid as possible. These type of reactions are characterised as
”mass transfer limited”. If instead ozone is applied in a pre-ozonation step for the
oxidation of biorefractory organic matter (organic matter exhibiting a low BOD/COD
ratio) the rate of ozone mass transfer should not be as rapid.
Here the most im-
portant element is maintaining a speciﬁc, and often lower, ozone concentration for a
very long period of time. Even in the presence of a large excess amout of ozone, the
rates of these type of reactions are slow, and hence they are ”reaction rate limited”.[90]
At this point, the initial ozone mole fraction and the bubble lifetime will be crucial
factors. The presence of a large amount of non-transferable gas in the nano- or micro-
bubble was predicted to sharply reduce the mass transfer rate. So the bubbles must
87
==PAGE:100==
8.3. MODEL 3
CHAPTER 8. MODEL ANALYSIS
contain a great percentage of ozone and have a shorter lifetime to obtain the requested
fast mass transfer in the disinfection process.
Conversely, the bubbles in the pre-
ozonation must initially contain a relatively small amount of ozone to prolong their life
time. The optimal ozone doses during the two treatment processes are illustrated by
the two schematic drawings in Figure 8.37 and 8.38.
Figure 8.37: Plot showing the optimal ozone dosage during
the course of the disinfection process.[90]
Figure 8.38: Plot showing the optimal ozone dosage during
the course of the pre-ozonation.[90]
8.3.10
Comparison of Model 3 and Model 2
For low concentrations of ozone, the mass transfer should not be associated with major
volume changes. So in this case the third mathematical model should approach the
second mathematical model where volume changes are neglected. This is tested by
carrying out simulations in which the mole fraction of ozone is set suﬃciently low. In
this test the radii are set as follows: R1 = 0.001m, R2 = 0.0011m, and R3 = 0.0012m.
This means that the two ﬁlm layers have the same thickness.
In Model 3 this is
accomplished by letting the the gas ﬁlm account for about 45% of the total volume.
The initial concentration of ozone is set to 0.0041 mol/m3, which corresponds to an
initial ozone mole fraction of 7.72 · 10−5. This is indeed a suﬃciently low mole fraction
to avoid volume changes of the bubble.
A list of all the parameters used in these
simulations are seen in Appendix T.
The results of Model 2 are seen in Figure 8.39, while the results of Model 3 are seen
in Figure 8.40.
As seen by looking at Figure 8.39 and Figure 8.40 the concentration proﬁles are iden-
tical for Model 2 and Model 3. This proves that the Model 3 approaches Model 2 for
low ozone concentrations as anticipated.
Since Model 2 and Model 3 provide the most realistic descriptions of the diﬀusion
process these two models will be the main focus in the rest of the report - especially
Model 3.

Comparison with Linear Models
Some of the concentration proﬁles in sections 7.2 and 7.3 became linear. This suggests
that the diﬀusion process from the bubbles mathematically could be described by using
linear models instead of the more advanced discretised models. It will now be tested
whether it is possible to approximate Model 2 and Model 3 with simple linear models.
This is done by comparing the results of these models with the results of similar but
linear models - Model 4 and Model 5.
9.1
Linearity of Model 2
First the linearity of Model 2 is tested. In order to do so the results of Model 2 and
Model 4 will be compared graphically. For this graphical analysis two types of plots
will be generated:
• A plot of the ozone concentration inside the bulk gas phase as a function of time.
• A plot of the ozone concentration as a function of the radius, at diﬀerent points
in time (the concentration proﬁles).
The plots will be made for two diﬀerent cases. In the two diﬀerent cases the dimensions
of the bulk gas phase and the ﬁlm layers are changed. Table 9.1 lists the the dimensions
and the c0,initial values which will be used to carry out the simulations. Here the bubble
size is scaled-down to more realistic values of micro-scale.

By looking at Figure 9.1 and 9.2 it is seen how Model 2 diﬀers more from Model 4
at high ozone concentrations, whereas at lower ozone concentrations the results of the
two models become much more alike. Initially, Model 2 decreases much faster to a
lower concentration than the linear model does. This diﬀerence must be related to the
non-linearity of Model 2. As described in section 8.2.2, the concentration proﬁles in
Model 2 become more linear as time passes by and the ozone concentration becomes
lower, which is the reason why the two graphs in Figure 9.1 and Figure 9.2 match each
other at lower concentrations.
The ﬁgures show that the dimensions of the bulk gas phase and the ﬁlm layers also
play an important role. The greater a proportion of the total volume the ﬁlm layers
occupy, the more the results of Model 2 and Model 4 diverge. If the ﬁlm layers make
up only a small percentage of the total volume then there is a better match between
the two models. In Case 1 the ﬁlm layers constitute 25% of the total volume, whereas
the bulk phase accounts for 75%. And in this case the curves of the two models are
very close to each other as seen in Figure 9.1. In Case 2 the ﬁlm layers occupy 75% of
the total volume. The bulk gas phase only takes up 25% of the total volume. In this
case the two curves are much further apart for a long period of time as seen in Figure
9.2.

CHAPTER 9. COMPARISON WITH LINEAR MODELS
In Figure 9.2, it is interesting to see that the linear model and the non-linear model
intersect at t = 0.97s, corresponding to a concentration of 0.25mol/m3. After this
point the linear model has overtaken the non-linear model and decreases slightly faster
than Model 2. The phenomenon that the linear model at some point overtakes the
non-linear model also exists in Case 1 (see Figure 9.1), although it is not as clear. In
Case 1 the graphs of Model 2 and Model 4 intersect at about t = 180ms, corresponding
to a concentration around 0.19mol/m3. The reason why the two curves intersect are
discussed in more detail in the following subsection.
9.1.2
Comparing the Concentration Proﬁles
The concentration proﬁles at diﬀerent points in time for Model 2 and Model 4 are
plotted in the same charts for each of the two cases. As the time increases the concen-
tration proﬁles of Model 4 change colour from purple to black, while the concentration
proﬁles of Model 2 changes from blue to red. Model 4 results are drawn with dashed
lines, whereas Model 2 is represented by full drawn lines. The plots are seen in Figure
9.3 and 9.4.
Figure 9.3: The concentration as a function of the radial position at diﬀerent points in time for Case
1. Model 2 is represented by the full drawn lines in blue and red, whereas Model 4 is represented by
the dashed purple and black lines.
The depiction of the concentration proﬁles makes it much easier to see what diﬀerence
it makes that the diﬀusion process happens through a linear rather than a non-linear
process. In fact, these plots also provide a good representation of the ﬂuxes at diﬀerent
points in time. Generally, the non-linearity of Model 2 results in a much higher ﬂux of
92
==PAGE:105==
9.1. LINEARITY OF MODEL 2
CHAPTER 9. COMPARISON WITH LINEAR MODELS
Figure 9.4: The concentration as a function of the radial position at diﬀerent points in time for case

material out of the bubble in the ﬁrst period of time in both cases. However, over time
the non-linearity decreases as the ﬂux decreases. The ﬂux of the Model 4 also changes
as time goes by, with the concentration proﬁles being a bit steeper in the beginning.
Despite the changing ﬂuxes the linear model can be looked upon as representing an
’average ﬂux’. This is also the reason why the two graphs of Model 2 and Model 4
intersect, and that the linear model is slower and faster, respectively, than Model 2 on
both sides of this intersection.
The diﬀerences between Case 1 and Case 2 are even more noticeable in these plots.
In Figure 9.3 (representing Case 1) the two graphs match perfectly at t = 180ms and
onwards. Also at t = 72ms the diﬀerences are not so pronounced. In Figure 9.4 there
is a huge diﬀerence between the two models, and the concentration proﬁles are very
far apart most of the time. The concentration proﬁles match at t = 1s.
Based on the above analysis the linear model seems perfectly capable of describing the
diﬀusion process from micro- or nano-bubbles assuming that the ﬁlm layers are very
thin and that the volume of the bubble does not change. However, if the ﬁlm is thick
then Model 4 is inadequate.

9.2
Linearity of Model 3
Model 3 approximates Model 2 at low amounts of ozone. Therefore, it is to be ex-
pected that there are similar trends regarding linearity as described above. Only if the
ﬁlm layer is thin will a linear model give a good description of the diﬀusion process.
However, Model 3 has an additional feature in that the volume shrinks over time. So
it is possible that the shrinkage aﬀects whether the model can be approximated by a
linear model. In order to test this, the results of Model 3 and Model 5 will be compared.
Model 3 and Model 5 will be compared graphically. Plots showing the concentration
inside the bulk gas phase as a function of time will be plotted for diﬀerent ozone
mole fractions. Two diﬀerent cases will be examined as shown in Table 9.2. In these
simulations the initial dimensions of the bubble are not changed from Case 1 to Case
2. The only parameter which is varied is the mole fraction of ozone. It is a deliberate
choice that the ﬁlm layers are very thin. This leads to greater linearity of the discretized
model. In this analysis, the ﬁlm layers represent only 6% of the total volume.

bubble starts to shrink, there is a very large diﬀerence between the two models. In
Figure 9.6 it is seen how the amount of moles of ozone decreases much faster for the
non-linear model than for the linear one. The reason for the big diﬀerence between the
models is partly because the non-linearity of Model 3 increases when XO3,initial increases,
as mentioned in section 8.3.9. At the same time, a reduction in radius (resulting in
a smaller average bubble size) does not lead to an equally large change in the ﬂux or
the rate of mass transfer for the two models. In Model 5 the bubble lifetime declines
rapidly as a function of the initial ozone mole fraction. For the linear model the bubble
lifetime also decreases as a function XO3,initial. However, this happens at a much slower
rate. Figure 9.7 shows tlife as a function of XO3,initial for Model 3 and Model 5. In this
ﬁgure XO3,initial is varied from 0.00001 to 0.95. Both the horizontal axis as well as the
vertical axis use a logarithmic scale. tlife of Model 5 was 51ms at XO3,initial = 0.001,
while at XO3,initial = 0.9 the lifetime had decreased to 34ms. Similarly, the bubble
lifetime for Model 3 dropped from 30.11ms to 0.10ms.

Up to now there are still some parameters that are set arbitrarily in the models: 1)
the diﬀusion coeﬃcient in the gas ﬁlm, 2) the ﬁlm thicknesses, and 3) the bubble size.
As shown in the model analysis chapter, these parameters have an inﬂuence on the
ﬁnal diﬀusion process and on the degree of linearity. So in the following chapters,
these input parameters to the models will be determined using correlations and doing
experimental work.
95
==PAGE:108==
Gas Diﬀusivity
The ﬁrst input parameter, which is determined, is the diﬀusivity in the gas ﬁlm. This
parameter depends on the type of inert gases mixing with the ozone in the bubble. An
ozone generator is used in the production of the bubbles. Both pure oxygen and air can
be used as input streams in ozone generators to produce O3. Usually, a greater amount
of ozone is obtained when using pure oxygen as input. However, the bubble generator
will never be able to convert oxygen 100% to ozone. Liming Hu and Zhiram Xia (2018)
have carried out experiments with micro- and nano-bubbles using two diﬀerent types
of ozone generators.
In their experiments the resulting gas stream from the ozone
generator contained about 3% to 7% ozone.[6] The rest was oxygen.
The bubbles
generated with Norlex’ ozone generator will probably also contain about this amount
of ozone and a remaining amount of oxygen.
In the whole project it is assumed that the diﬀusivity of ozone in the gas ﬁlm is equal
to that in pure oxygen (so that Dgf = Dg). Studies made by Chen and Othmer (1962)
show that the following equation can be used to calculate the diﬀusivity of O3 in pure
oxygen[91]:

T0 is the standard temperature [K].
Equation (10.1) is applicable at conditions near standard temperature (= 273.15K)
and pressure (1atm). It is assessed that the equation can provide a good estimate of
the diﬀusion coeﬃcient of ozone at 298.15K and 1atm.

10.1
Model Results
The diﬀusivity of 1.45·10−5m2/s is about 10,000 times greater than the value previously
used (1.89·10−9m2/s). Therefore, it is guaranteed that this change will greatly aﬀect the
concentration proﬁles. Then a new simulation is carried out in order to see how much
the results change by setting the diﬀusivity in the ﬁrst ﬁlm layer to this value. The
bubble is set to be a micro-scaled bubble. Furthermore, realistic diﬀusion coeﬃcients
are used in both the gas ﬁlm and the liquid ﬁlm. The bubble’s ozone content is set
to 3%. The gas ﬁlm is set to constitute 20% of the overall ﬁlm volume-wise (as it is
known from literature studies that the ﬁlm thickness of the gas ﬁlm is far less than the
thickness of the liquid ﬁlm). A list of all the parameters used in this simulation is seen
in Appendix V. The resulting plots of the concentration as a function of the radius at
diﬀerent times are shown in Figure 10.1.

As suggested in section 8.1.4, the concentration proﬁles in one ﬁlm layer become more
linear when the diﬀusion coeﬃcient is higher in that layer relative to in the other ﬁlm
layer. Here the diﬀusion in the gas ﬁlm is so rapid that the concentration proﬁles simply
become ﬂat lines all the way. So the liquid ﬁlm is without doubt the rate-limiting part
of the system. This results in that one can completely ignore the gas ﬁlm in the mass
transfer calculations. This result is very important for the entire understanding of the
overall mass transfer process. It shows, that one should consider the bubble system
97
==PAGE:110==
10.2. SINGLE-FILM MODEL
CHAPTER 10. GAS DIFFUSIVITY
in a completely diﬀerent way than what currently has been done in the project. The
system now theoretically only consists of a bulk gas phase surrounded by a liquid ﬁlm.
10.2
Single-Film Model
It is tempting to believe that the mathematical models developed are no longer equally
useful, since they are based on a completely diﬀerent understanding of the bubble
system. However, the same mathematical models and their associated MatLab scripts
(as described in this project) can just as easily be used when there is only one ﬁlm layer
instead of two. For this one does not have to derive brand new models or make major
changes in the MatLab codes. The easiest way to transform Model 3 into a single-ﬁlm
system is to adjust the boundary condition for the ﬁrst volume element, and then to
set the parameter values in the MatLab scripts completely diﬀerent than before. A
brief review of these changes will now follow.
First of all R1 is now to be considered as the inner boundary of the liquid ﬁlm, while R3
is the outer boundary of the ﬁlm. The partitioning coeﬃcient Hcc should be set equal
to 1, which indicates that there is no phase transition at R2 (which is now positioned
in the middle of the ﬁlm layer). However, in this new bubble set-up the gas-liquid-
interface is moved from R2 to R1. There is a phase transition directly from the bulk
gas phase to the liquid ﬁlm with Hcc,new = 0.29. Thus, the amount of ozone diﬀusing
out of the bulk gas phase per time unit is now dependent on the solubility of ozone in
the surrounding liquid. So the neighbouring volume element to compartment 1 in the
discretization scheme has to be the new interface. Now the ozone concentration inside
the bubble has to be calculated using the following diﬀerential equation:
dn0
dt = 16 · π2 · Dgf · R1(t)4
∆V
·(c1 −Hcc,new · c0) = 16 · π2 · Dgf · R1(t)4

The next model input parameter to be determined is the ﬁlm thickness and then the

98
==PAGE:111==
Film Thickness
In section 10 it was concluded that one could ignore the gas ﬁlm, as the diﬀusion is
much faster in the gas ﬁlm relative to the liquid ﬁlm. Therefore, it is not necessary to
determine the thickness of the gas ﬁlm layer as it is irrelevant to the diﬀusion process.
As mentioned in section 6.6.3 theoretical correlations with the Sherwood number can
be used to determine the ﬁlm thickness of the liquid ﬁlm. Figure 11.1 shows a plot of
the Sherwood number as a function of the Reynolds number for mass transfer from a
sphere. In Figure 11.1 the circles are experimental data points, whereas the two curves
indicate the results of two diﬀerent mass transfer equations: 1) the Garner & Suckling
equation, and 2) the Fr¨ossling equation.[92]
Figure 11.1: Mass Transfer data for spheres. The circles indicate experimental data, whereas the
dashed line is results obtained by the Garner & Suckling equation and the full line is the results
obtained by the Fr¨ossling equation. The ﬁgure is modiﬁed from [92].
Several literary sources like Worden et al. (1998) [89], Naoea et al (2008) [93], and
Painmanakul et al. (2009) [94] state that the Sherwood number approaches 2 when
the Reynolds number approaches zero, as seen in Figure 11.1. So according to the
99
==PAGE:112==
CHAPTER 11. FILM THICKNESS
researchers the following correlation applies to a sphere in a stagnant medium:
Sh = klf · db
Dlf
= 2
(11.1)
The mass transfer coeﬃcient is assumed to be given by:
klf = Dlf
δlf
(11.2)
If Equation (11.1) and (11.2) are combined this leads to:
Sh = db

= 2 ⇒δlf = db
2
(11.3)
As seen in Equation (11.3) the liquid ﬁlm thickness is related to the bubble diameter.
In fact the thickness is half of the bubble diameter. This means the bigger the bubble
the larger the ﬁlm layer. Intuitively, one would expect that the ﬁlm layer around the
bubble would be a lot thinner. This may be because many studies of bubbles and ﬁlm
thicknesses are based on larger bubbles in motion. The movement of a bubble tend to
reduce the ﬁlm thickness.[95] Even if the micro-bubble in the mathematical models was
described as being in motion, the rise velocity would be so low that it would probably
not aﬀect the ﬁlm thickness very much (ub = 50µm/s for a 10µm bubble).[58]
As the ﬁlm thickness depends on the size of the bubble, the next step will be to ﬁgure
out how big the bubbles produced by Norlex’s bubble set-up really are. The size of
the bubble has to be determined experimentally. So the next chapter will contain a
description of the experimental work that was performed.
100
==PAGE:113==
Experiments
As mentioned earlier, only a limited amount of knowledge has been documented in
literature regarding experimental work on ozonation processes with tiny bubbles. So
Norlex’s research work on micro- and nano-bubbles requires that a lot of experiments
are performed.
The company’s ﬁrst important task is to establish a solid method
for generating bubbles of micro- and/or nano-scale. Norlex also has to prove that the
bubbles have the size they advertise. The experiments in this project has two purposes:
1) to clearly demonstrate that it is possible by simple methods to form as small bubbles
as Norlex wishes to use, and 2) to determine the bubble size to use as model parameter.
So in this project the experimental work consists of two separate parts:
1. Formation of micro- and/or nano-bubbles using Norlex’s bubble generator set-up.
2. Measurement of the size of the bubbles obtained in part 1 of the experimental
process.
Both parts of the experiment will be described in detail in this chapter. Photographs
of the experimental equipment used can be seen in Appendix W.
12.1
Generate Tiny Bubbles
This section will describe the experimental set-up and equipment best applied to gen-
erate tiny ozone containing bubbles. It is advantageous for Norlex if the experimental
set-up is relatively simple and does not require too expensive equipment. In the follow-
ing subsection various ways of generating micro- and nano-bubbles will be introduced
to give an overview of the experimental options at hand.
12.1.1
Governing Principles Behind the Generation of Tiny Bubbles
Cavitation is the process by which bubbles develop within a liquid. It occurs when a
liquid is subjected to rapid changes in local pressure. For bubbles to form the local
pressure should drop far below the saturated vapour pressure. At this point the tensile
strength1 of the liquid will be exceeded, thereby creating an explosive growth in the
number of gaseous bubbles.[96]
There are two types of cavitation of experimental relevance: 1) acoustic cavitation and
2) hydrodynamic cavitation.
1resistance of a material to breaking under tension
101
==PAGE:114==
12.1. GENERATE TINY BUBBLES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
Acoustic Cavitation
In acoustic cavitation an external excitation, such as shock waves or ultrasonic waves,
induce cavitation locally. So bubbles are generated from gas pockets in the liquid when
the rarefaction waves propagate throughout the liquid region.[97]
Hydrodynamic Cavitation
Hydrodynamic cavitation occurs, when the pressure is reduced by the hydrodynamic
motion of the ﬂuid. The hydrodynamic motion is in turn aﬀected by the geometrical
layout of the ﬂow passages. Bernoulli’s equation states that the required pressure re-
duction results either from augmenting the gravitational energy or the kinetic energy
of the liquid. An increase in the gravitational energy is caused in a piping system when
the pipe elevation increases locally. The kinetic energy rises when there is an abrupt
reduction in the cross-section of the ﬂow passage.[96]
The commercial bubble generators available today are based on the physical principles
above. In the next subsection two types of bubble generators will be shortly described.
12.1.2
Bubble Generators
So far only few experimental methods have been developed for generation of micro- and
nano-bubbles. Generators based on acoustic cavitation are often deselected in favour
of generators based on hydrodynamic cavitation. This is because the acoustic methods
are more expensive and require a more complex pipe and tank system than the other
methods. According to literary sources the two most common type of generators ap-
plied to produce tiny bubbles are: 1) decompression type generators, and 2) gas-liquid
circulation type generators.[51]
The Decompression Method
In the decompression method supersaturated conditions for gas dissolution is created
at pressures around 304-405kPa. At such high pressures the gas will be highly un-
stable. When the pressurized gas is introduced into the water tank there will be a
drastic pressure drop of the supersaturated gas. This results in the generation of a
large amount of tiny bubbles (with sizes of micro- or nano-scale).[98]
The Gas-Liquid Circulation Method
The principle of the gas-liquid circulation method is that gas is introduced into a
water vortex. This means that the water will be ﬁlled with larger gas bubbles, which
are subsequently broken down into much tinier bubbles by breaking up the vortex.
The experimental set-up of a typical gas-water circulation generator can be seen in the
article by Agarwal et al (2011) [51].
Generally, the generation of tiny ozone bubbles through decompression should be much
more eﬃcient than through gas-liquid circulation.[51] Therefore, the decompression
method is used in the bubble experiments at Norlex.
102
==PAGE:115==
12.1. GENERATE TINY BUBBLES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
12.1.3
The Generation of Bubbles Using Norlex’s Experimental Set-Up
The experimental set-up of the bubble experiments at Norlex is shown in Figure 12.1.
Figure 12.1: A schematic drawing of the experimental set-up following the decompression method.
The formation of bubbles is a multi-step process. To begin with a tub is ﬁlled with
approx. 30L of water. It is then placed inside a fume cupboard, because ozone later
will be added to the water and leave the liquid phase as toxic gas. The tub is connected
to a pump and a small reactor by hoses. The mixing pump takes in the surrounding
air through an air-inlet and creates a negative pressure which causes the mixing of air
and water. The outlet stream of the pump enters the dissolution tank in which an
impeller is installed to further ensure a good mixing between liquid and air. Generally,
the dissolution tank will provide storage space for the gas-liquid-mixture.
It is important to carefully balance the water inlet stream and the incoming air ﬂow.
If the inlet stream of water is too large there is no room for adding air. So often the
water ﬂow is lowered while the air ﬂow is revised upwards to achieve the right condi-
tions for mixing. The water is now circulated from the tub to the pump and then into
the small dissolution tank and back to the tub. No bubbles are created yet by this
circulation of aerated water. The bubbles will be formed by passing the ﬂow through
a nozzle submerged in the tub of water. So a nozzle is screwed onto the hose which
pours aerated water into the tub. This narrows the water ﬂow so much that the out-
let pressure increases signiﬁcantly. Usually, the pressure becomes around 0.3-0.4MPa.
When the water is pushed out it experiences a great pressure drop, which induces the
formation of bubbles. Here the design of the nozzle plays a crucial role. It determines
the pressure drop, which in turn determines the size of the bubbles. When the bubbles
start to form using the nozzle from Norlex the water will obtain a milk-like colour as
103
==PAGE:116==
12.1. GENERATE TINY BUBBLES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
seen in Figure 12.2. This change in colour suggests that the generated bubbles most
likely are micro-bubbles and not nano-bubbles.
Figure 12.2: A photograph of the water in which tiny bubbles are continuously generated.
The
photograph shows the cloudy colour of the water.
So far the bubbles do not yet contain any ozone. It is a good idea to ensure a solid
production of bubbles before ozone is added. In order to add ozone to the bubbles a lab
ozone generator from Triogen is used. In this machine ozone is produced when either
dry air or pure oxygen gas is passed over a ceramic dielectric of an ozone generating
module. The ozone output varies depending on the feed gases. The machine can pro-
duce up to 4g/h of O3 using air and 10g/h of O3 using pure oxygen. The generator is
capable of producing ozone concentrations up to 10vol%.[99] In the experiments pure
oxygen is used as the feed gas, which results in an O3 dosage of 7.5g/h. The outlet of
the ozone generator is connected to the rest of the system through a thin plastic hose.
Instead of taking in air from the surroundings the hose now provides ozone, which
mixes with water in the system with the pump and the small dissolution tank.
When the generation of bubbles and ozone is stopped, by switching oﬀall the equip-
ment, the water remains milky white for some time. Then the water again becomes
transparent because the bubbles either burst or rise to the surface. Maybe the micro-
bubbles generate nano-bubbles as they burst, but this is uncertain.
In order to test whether the bubbles really came to contain ozone, the ozone concen-
tration in the water was measured.
12.1.4
Measurement of the Ozone Concentration
In order to measure the ozone concentration in the water, during and after the gen-
eration of bubbles, simple colorimetric tests can be applied. For these tests a MD610
photometer from ”Lovibond Water Testing” is used. This photometer is capable of
measuring important parameters for water analysis and gives ﬂexibility of choice for
many tests. The device can do measurements on a lot of components from aluminium
to zinc.[100] The device’s test range of ozone is 0.02 to 2mg/L.[101]
104
==PAGE:117==
12.2. MEASUREMENT OF THE BUBBLE SIZES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
Before the photometer can be used to do an actual measurement, a reference test needs
to be carried out. In order to do so some of the ozonated water from the tub is added
to a small test tube, which is inserted in the photometer. After the reference measure-
ment the majority of the water in the test tube is poured out. There is only water
enough left in the tube to ease the grinding of two DPD tablets from Lovibond Water
Testing. The tablets are denoted DPD No. 1 and DPD No. 3, and one of each should
be added to the sample. The chemical reagent N,N-diethyl-p-phenyldiamine (DPD)
is added, because it reacts with ozone to produce a colour. The colour intensity is
directly proportional to the amount of ozone in a sample.[102] When the tablets have
been crushed suﬃciently and dissolved in the remaining water, ozonated water from
the tub is added to the test tube. Ozone oxidizes the DPD to form a magenta coloured
compound.[102] Figure 12.3 shows a sample, which has turned magenta due to the
presence of ozone.
Figure 12.3: The sample turning magenta because of the reaction between ozone and DPD.
The photometer measures the intensity of the colour and converts this measurement
into a concentration reading. It is important to note that chlorine residues and impu-
rities in the water may interfere with the readings. Therefore, there is greater uncer-
tainties associated with using tap water instead of using deionized water. This is also
the reason why deionized was used in these experiments.
It has now been proven that it is easy to make small bubbles containing ozone using
relatively simple equipment. Now the question is whether the bubbles are within the
right size interval to classify them as micro- or nano-bubbles.
12.2
Measurement of the Bubble Sizes
This section will describe the experimental equipment used in order to measure the
sizes of micro- and nano-bubbles.
Emphasis will be put on describing the speciﬁc
procedure used in the experiments. This ﬁrst subsection begins with a description of
the underlying principles of a laser diﬀraction particle size analyser.
105
==PAGE:118==
12.2. MEASUREMENT OF THE BUBBLE SIZES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
12.2.1
The Principles of a Laser Diﬀraction Particle Size Analyser
For relatively large bubbles (mill-scaled) size measurement is possible by image process-
ing. This technique involves using an optical microscope and a video camera. However,
for bubbles of a size of only a few µm in diameter it can be diﬃcult to use this type
of equipment. Instead a laser diﬀraction particle size analyser is applied to measure
the size distribution of micro- and nano-bubbles in a medium. The method of laser
diﬀraction is based on the relationship between light and surfaces. When light hits a
surface it either gets: 1) diﬀracted/scattered, 2) refracted, 3) reﬂected, or 4) absorbed
as illustrated in Figure 12.4.[103]
Figure 12.4: The types of interaction between light and a surface.[103]
So as Figure 12.4 illustrates, when particles are irradiated with a laser beam, the light
is diﬀracted in various directions away from those particles. A ﬁxed spatial pattern
is formed in the direction the light is scattered.[104] This diﬀraction light pattern de-
pends on the particle sizes. The laser diﬀraction analyser relates the particle size to the
angle and intensity of the scattered light. The light is scattered more intensely and at
smaller angles oﬀlarge particles than small particles. So the particle size is measured
indirectly.[105, 103]
This technique of laser diﬀraction is intended for the measurement of the size distribu-
tion of solid particles. There is considerable doubt as to how laser radiation aﬀects and
interacts with bubbles. So it may not even be certain that this technique can provide
any exact knowledge about the bubble sizes. However, the use of a laser diﬀraction
particle size analyser to measure the bubble size distributions has been proven success-
ful according to researchers such as Sato (2005) and Sennoga et al. (2012).[56, 106]
106
==PAGE:119==
12.2. MEASUREMENT OF THE BUBBLE SIZES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS
12.2.2
Experimental Procedure
In this project a ”Mastersizer 3000” laser diﬀraction particle size analyser from ”Malvern”
is applied to carry out the experiments. This analyser has a size range from 10nm to
3.5mm with a measurement accuracy of 0.6%.[107]2
In the beginning of the experimental work one starts to add clean deionized water to
the dispersion unit of the particle size analyser. This is done in order to initialise the
measuring instrument properly. When the system is initialised one has to measure the
background scattering from the dispersant. In this case a background measurement is
just carried out on deionized water. A bubble sample is prepared at the same time.
Afterwards the actual measurement can be carried out. So the bubble sample is added
to the dispersion unit and one clicks ’Start Measurement’ on the computer. The scat-
tering data is recorded using only a blue light source. This is because blue light is
diﬀracted more by very small particles than red light.[108] Usually, about ﬁve repeat
measurements are done on each sample in order to assess the stability of the sample.
Once this is ﬁnished, the scattering data is analysed by the computer software to de-
termine a volume based bubble size distribution. In other words the bubble size will
be reported as a volume equivalent sphere diameter.
12.2.3
Software Settings
Because the machine originally is designed for measuring of solid particles the need
to select the right settings in the computer software becomes very important. Dur-
ing the measurements the real part of the refraction index is set to 1 in the optical
mode, while the imaginary part is set to zero. These are the typical refractive indexes
of gasses.[109] Due to the fact that bubbles are gaseous, the real number portion of
the complex refractive index is smaller than that of water (real part of the refractive
index of water is 1.33). Usually, the real part of the refractive index of solid particles
is greater than the water index. This naturally causes a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in the
light intensity data of the diﬀracted light. This diﬀerence can possibly also be used to
diﬀerentiate the bubbles from minor solid impurities in the water.
Apart from entering the correct value of the refractive index, one also has to check
other settings like the stirrer speed, and the duration of measurement. The sizes of the
bubbles generated by Norlex’s bubble generator were measured in two experimental
sessions. The two sessions diﬀered from each other because the experimental settings
were diﬀerent as seen in Table 12.1.
The impeller in the dispersion unit, which stirs the samples, was the ﬁrst thing varied
in the two experimental sessions. In the ﬁrst experimental session the stirrer speed was
set to default, which is 2500rpm.[107] In the second session the stirrer agitation was
reduced from 2500rpm to 1000rpm. There is doubt as to whether a rapid agitation
will destroy the bubbles. This was the reason for reducing the rotational speed of the
2The accuracy is deﬁned for the measurement of monomodal latex standards.[107]
107
==PAGE:120==
12.2. MEASUREMENT OF THE BUBBLE SIZES
CHAPTER 12. EXPERIMENTS

stirrer.
In order to remove excess gas, the system was automatically degassed prior to the
ﬁrst experimental session. In the second experiment this degas function was turned
oﬀ. Degassing the system before carrying out the size measurements creates a delay
between sampling and measurement. Actually, all the bubbles may have disappeared
before the actual size measurement begins. This concern is also the motivation for
reducing the duration of measurement from 20 seconds to 5 seconds from the ﬁrst to
the second experiment. The duration of measurement indicates how long time it takes
to carry out each of the ﬁve repeat measurements in a single run.
The rest of the computer settings are kept as default in both experiments. Prior to
measuring the bubble sizes in various bubble samples, a size measurement on deionized
water is carried out in each of the two sessions.
108
==PAGE:121==
Experimental Results
The experiments have now been conducted and the results have been obtained. In
this chapter the results of the diﬀerent experiments will be carefully analysed. In each
analysis the average of the ﬁve repeat measurements for each bubble sample has been
applied, instead of analysing all ﬁve repeat measurements individually for each sample.
13.0.1
Volume Distribution Curves
Before the start of the experiments it was uncertain whether it was possible to measure
the size of bubbles using a laser diﬀraction particle size analyser. However, there are
several elements in the results obtained from the experiments which conﬁrm that the
machine can actually measure the sizes of the bubbles.
Firstly, the volume distribution curves of deionized water with and without bubbles
diﬀer signiﬁcantly. The volume distribution curve for deionized water without bubbles
are seen in Figure 13.1, while the distribution curves of the bubble samples are seen
in Figure 13.2. All the distribution curves in Figure 13.1 and Figure 13.2 represent
the results of the second experimental session. The volume distribution curves of the
bubble samples in the ﬁrst experiment are seen in Appendix X.
It is clear to see that the samples containing deionized water with the bubbles repre-
sent a much narrower size interval than the samples with no bubbles. For the sample
without bubbles the volume distribution curve spreads over sizes of 0µm to 400µm,
and peaks around 163µm. The volume distribution curves of the bubble samples peak
below 100µm.
Secondly, the volume distributions of the bubble samples vary as seen by the curves
in Figure 13.1. The measurements do not result in sharp peaks. Instead they cre-
ate smooth curves, which all peak at diﬀerent points. Assume the results were error
measurements, and the particle size analyser did not record anything, then the volume
distribution curves would probably be uniform with a very sharp peak at one particular
point.
Thirdly, all measurements on the bubble samples are within the same size range. This
is a good sign, as it is expected that the bubbles are about the same order of magnitude
in all the samples. In the next subsection a statistical analysis of the results will be
performed in order to determine the mean bubble size of all the bubble samples.
109
==PAGE:122==

bubble sample: 1) the mean bubble size, 2) the standard deviation, and 3) the coeﬃ-
cient of variation.
The mean bubble size in each of the bubble samples is calculated by using the formula

Mean Bubble Diameter
As seen in Table 13.1, when it comes to the mean diameter, there is no big diﬀerence
between the results of the ﬁrst and the second experimental session. Only sample 1.2
and sample 2.1 diﬀer from the rest. The three other samples (1.1, 2.2, and 2.3) have
mean diameters close to 50µm.
The reason for the lower mean diameter of sample 2.1 may be due to the fact that
it took too long time before the actual size measurement was started. This gave the
bubbles enough time to shrink in size (or to disappear from the water). The reason for
the higher mean diameter in sample 1.2 is likely due to other unknown experimental
errors. Perhaps the bubbles did become larger due to coalescence when they ﬂowed
through the particle size analyser.
There is no clear indication that it makes a signiﬁcant diﬀerence to use the settings
of session 2 instead of the ones in session 1 - at least not when it comes to the mean
diameter. Due to the consistent results between experimental session 1 and 2 the ma-
jority of the bubbles do not seem to break due to agitation. Instead the bubbles are
111
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 13. EXPERIMENTAL RESULTS
thought to quickly follow the water ﬂow. The degas function does not seem to aﬀect
the results either.
In the ﬁrst experimental round the duration of measurement is much higher than in
the second session. Due to the longer processing time more bubbles should disappear
from the water, and it should not be possible to measure bubble sizes of the same
magnitude in all repeat measurements. So the last measurement of all ﬁve repeat mea-
surements should result in a signiﬁcantly lower bubble size compared to the results of
the ﬁrst repeat measurement. However, the bubble sizes did not change signiﬁcantly
during the ﬁve repeat measurements (each of 20 seconds). This is also reﬂected in the
average of all the measurements. There is a plausible explanation for this. The bub-
bles from the ﬁrst throughput get stuck on the glass window through which the laser
beams pass. When the bubbles stick to the glass surface they do not shrink in size.
So the machine will only measure the sizes of the bubbles of the ﬁrst throughput be-
cause they are stuck on the glass surface. This ’sticky’ behaviour of the bubbles is also
observed on the surfaces of the water tub, where the bubbles are initially generated.
On the surfaces of the water tub the bubbles remain stable for very long periods of time.
Standard Deviation
The standard deviation is used as a measure of the dispersion of a set of data around
its mean. If individual data points are further away from the mean and spread over
a wider range of values, this results in a higher standard deviation.[113] As seen in
Table 13.1 the lowest standard deviation is obtained in sample 2.1, and the second
lowest standard deviation is obtained in sample 1.1. The highest standard deviation is
obtained in sample 2.3, and the second highest in sample 2.2. Generally, the standard
deviation of the second experimental session is higher than in the ﬁrst session. There
are not bubbles of as large sizes in the samples with settings 1 as in the samples with
settings 2. This might indicate that some of the larger bubbles are destroyed by the
agitation of the impeller in the ﬁrst experiment. The rapid circulation in the dispersion
unit may also destabilize the bubbles causing them to shrink in size.
Coeﬃcient of Variation
It is advantageous to calculate the coeﬃcient of varation because it is dimensionless.
The CV value makes it possible to compare data sets with diﬀerent units of measure-
ment or widely diﬀerent mean values. Generally, the higher the coeﬃcient of variation,
the greater the level of dispersion around the mean.[114, 115] Sample 2.1 has the least
variation relative to its mean, while sample 2.3 has the most variation relative to its
mean. In sample 2.3 the variance of the standard deviation is 76% of the average, which
is a very high degree of deviation. When a CV value exceeds about 30% it usually
indicates problems in the data or that the experiment is not performed correctly.[115]
In some way it is expected that the CV value is higher as it is extremely diﬃcult to
obtain uniform micro- and/or nano-bubble sizes. In that case the level of dispersion
around the mean will be high. Moreover, there are deﬁnitely some experimental un-
certainties associated with measuring bubble sizes using a laser diﬀraction particle size
analyser. It is not the ideal measuring equipment to measure bubble sizes, which is
112
==PAGE:125==
CHAPTER 13. EXPERIMENTAL RESULTS
also reﬂected in the CV value. But, although the results are not that accurate, and the
experimental method is not the most suitable, one gets an indication of the bubble sizes.
The three missing input parameters for the mathematical model have now been deter-
mined: 1) The diﬀusion coeﬃcient in the gas ﬁlm is 1.45 · 10−5m2/s, which makes it
sensible to use a single-ﬁlm model rather than a two-ﬁlm model; 2) The diameter of
a single bubble is 50µm; 3) the liquid ﬁlm layer has a thickness of 25µm. In the next
chapter a ﬁnal realistic mass transfer process is presented.
113
==PAGE:126==
Realistic Mass Transfer Process
It is now possible to simulate a more realistic situation and get a better representation
of the actual mass transfer process. In this ﬁnal simulation a one-ﬁlm approach is
applied using the parameters in Table 14.1.
Table 14.1: The values of the parameters used in the ﬁnal simulation.

Initially the value of R3 is set to be twice as big as R1. But this diﬀerence between
R1 and R3 will not be kept throughout the entire diﬀusion process. As mentioned in
section 7.3.2, Model 3 is not set up to maintain a constant distance between the radii.
During the shrinkage process the radius of R1 is reduced 1.49µm, resulting in a ﬁnal
radius of 23.51µm. Simultaneously, R3 only changes 0.50µ, leading to a ﬁnal radius
of 49.65µm. This means that in the end R3 is 2.11 times greater than R1. Perhaps it
would have been more representative of the real situation to maintain a constant ratio
between the bubble radius and the ﬁlm thickness. However, this is uncertain.
The concentration as a function of the radius at diﬀerent points in time are plotted
in Figure 14.1 for this ﬁnal simulation. Generally, Model 3 predicts that the mass
transfer from a 50µm bubble is a rapid evolving and dynamic process. Based on this
simulation the lifetime1 of a bubble of size 50µm is 136ms. As seen in Figure 14.1 the
predicted time required for complete mass transfer through the liquid ﬁlm layer and
into the bulk liquid is 306ms. This is more clearly illustrated in Figure 14.2 showing
the concentration as a function of time in the last volume element in the discretization
scheme.
The bubble is emptied surprisingly quickly achieving a rapid mass transfer. It will

It should be pointed out that there is a dichotomy between the theoretical results of
this ﬁnal simulation and the experimental observations. In the experiments the bubbles
lingered in the water for several seconds. This suggests that there are some important
physical factors that the mathematical model does not account for. It will be a future
task to include these missing factors in the model.

==PAGE:128==
Discussion
Two-Film Versus Single-Film
Many scientiﬁc articles such as [79], [49], and [116] state that a single-ﬁlm model is
preferable to use. Then why in this project was a two-ﬁlm model initially developed.
This was done intentionally for the following reasons. First of all this helped to get
a fundamental understanding of the important mechanisms of the mass transfer pro-
cess between two phases.
Secondly, one got a better insight into the reasons why
the gas ﬁlm can be neglected, and how this is reﬂected on the concentration proﬁles.
Thirdly, from a mathematical point of view, it was interesting to demonstrate how
to set up diﬀerential equations describing a two-ﬁlm system. With few modiﬁcations
it may be possible to apply the same mathematical set-up on other systems, where
there is also mass transfer between two phases. The mathematics might be used in
polymer systems, in liquid-liquid systems and to describe mass transport from droplets.
Reasons for the Mass Transfer Becoming too Fast
Simulations using the ﬁnal model set-up indicated that the mass transfer was extremely
quick. Generally, there was a conﬂict between what was experimentally observed and
what was predicted by the ﬁnal mathematical model. There are several short-comings
in Model 3, which contribute to the theoretical mass transfer becoming too fast.
Firstly, good boundary conditions are vital in order to obtain a correct description of
the bubble system. In all the mathematical models in this project there is actually not
a good description of the outer boundary of the liquid ﬁlm. Presently, this boundary
is set to be an inﬁnite sink of material. In reality, the concentration in the bulk liquid
will rise until a certain level of saturation. As ozone accumulates in the bulk liquid
it will reduce the rate of diﬀusion from the bubble to the liquid. This boundary is
changed by assigning a well-deﬁned volume of the bulk liquid to the bubble. Then one
should establish an expression which takes into account the accumulation of ozone in
this volume of ﬂuid. This expression will most likely resemble the expression describ-
ing the ozone concentration in the bulk gas phase. Furthermore, one can combine this
equation with a ﬁrst order decay expression, that takes into account the simultaneous
decomposition of ozone in the water.[6]
Secondly, smaller bubbles are greatly aﬀected and stabilized by contaminants in the
water. Model 3 does not account for the mass transfer resistance of surfactants. Sebba
(1988) proposed a diﬀerent bubble set-up, where the bubbles were surrounded by both
a liquid ﬁlm layer and a surfactant-stabilized aqueous shell.[81] Amiri and Woodburn
(1990) estimated that the surfactant shell around a 35µm spherical bubble would be
116
==PAGE:129==
CHAPTER 15. DISCUSSION
about 0.75µm thick.[89] So a two-ﬁlm model with a liquid ﬁlm and a surfactant shell
might be something to consider for further model improvements.
With the exception of the boundary condition at R3, the assumptions made in the
development of Model 3 are well suited for a preliminary study of the dynamics of
nano- and micro-bubble mass transfer. The model describes many of the most essen-
tial elements of the mass transfer process.
Neglected Mass Transfer Factors
Some parameters were deliberately neglected in this ﬁrst description of the mass trans-
fer from a bubble. Two of these factors are the upward movement of the bubble, and
the eﬀects of surface tension.
It is a reasonable assumption not to include the upward movement of the bubble,
as nano- and micro-bubbles theoretically should be very little aﬀected by buoyancy.
However, in future work it would be sensible to examine, if this really is the case and
whether the slow movement has an impact on the ﬁlm thickness.
For bubbles it is well-known that the surface tension can eﬀect their internal pressure.[81]
This mechanism was also neglected in Model 3. The surface tension (σ) is a function
of the bubble curvature. As the bubble shrinks the surface curvature increases. This
in turn increases the internal pressure of the bubble.[117] The eﬀect of this can be
mathematically described by combining Henry’s law and the Young-Laplace equation
as follows[89]:
cNi,lf = Hcc · XO3 ·

Pl + 4σ
db

Rgas · T
(15.1)
The eﬀect of the pressure, generated by the surface tension, should also be included in
the expression for how much the volume of the bubble shrinks as a function of time.
There is still doubt about the validity of the Young-Laplace equation and whether the
internal pressure actually increases for bubbles of micro- and nano-scale. Nagayama et
al. (2006) state that the Young-Laplace equation most likely is inadequate in describing
micro- and nano-bubbles, because there are too few vapour atoms inside the bubbles
to support a force balance with the liquid.[118] Tomlan (1949) has also questioned the
eﬀects of the surface tension. So it is probably acceptable not to include this eﬀect in
a ﬁrst description of the diﬀusion process.[119]
Improvements Considering the Application of the Bubbles
Considering the context in which these mathematical models are to be used, the model
set-up can be made more useful and dynamic. This is done by including some of the
factors that aﬀect the solubility of ozone in water. Henry’s constant, and thus the
gas solubility, strongly depends on temperature, pH and ionic strength.
It is very
likely that some of these factors will change throughout the wastewater treatment pro-
cess. Firstly, the solubility of ozone decreases with increasing temperature. Secondly,
Henry’s coeﬃcient increases with the pH level. Thirdly, the gas solubility decreases
when the ionic strength grows. This eﬀect on ozone is especially evident when there are
phosphate, chloride or carbonate ions in the solution. Phosphate is one of the major
117
==PAGE:130==
CHAPTER 15. DISCUSSION
inorganic contaminants of wastewater (as described in section 6.2) so this might lower
the solubility considerably.[120, 121]
Experimental Procedure
In this project the particle size analyser measured the sizes of the bubbles reasonably
well. However, in the experiments one could have used other methods of measure-
ment. Sennoga et al. (2012) have attempted to measure the size of micro-bubbles
using three diﬀerent methods: laser diﬀraction (LD), electrical zone-sensing (ES), and
optical microscopy (OM). Apparently, there is a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the re-
sults obtained using LD relative to ES and OM. LD size measurements result in a much
lower mean bubble diameter. This is because the LD method is able to detect bubbles
of much smaller sizes compared to the other methods. Since Norlex wanted to generate
bubbles right down to the nano-bubble size, the LD was the best choice of method. It
is really uncertain whether micro-bubbles collapse and generate nano-bubbles.[106]
It should be noted that the accuracy of the size measurements using LD depends on
the accuracy of optical parameters such as: 1) the real and imaginary parts of the
refractive indices, 2) the medium in which the bubbles are suspended, and 3) the accu-
racy of information about the particle shape. If some of these parameters are wrongly
set, the size distribution measurements will be incorrect. Other methods (like Es and
OM) are not equally dependent on such model parameters. In this project the bubble
size experiments were performed as attentively as possible. The software settings were
chosen with great care.[106]
In this project the average bubble size turned out to be 50µm. Norlex cannot expect
to create bubbles of the same sizes in diﬀerent wastewater treatment processes. The
size, and the amount of bubbles generated will strongly depend on the liquid medium,
even when using same bubble generator and experimental conditions.[122] In all exper-
iments deionized water was used. Li et al. (2013) have experimentally shown that the
bubble size depends largely on the content of contaminants. In deionized water they
measured an average bubble size about 50µm. By adding 5mg/L surfactants, the aver-
age bubble size was reduced to 10µm.[123] So bubbles generated in actual wastewater
will probably be a lot smaller in size.
Above is described the factors in the mathematical model that leads to an inconsistency
between the experimental and the theoretical bubble lifetime. But some experimental
factors can also make the bubble lifetimes incomparable. The decomposition rate and
hence the ozone content in the bulk liquid will depend on the quality of the water.
The higher the quality of the water, the higher the half-life of ozone in the water.[124]
Using tap water instead of deionized water might have resulted in better consistency
between theory and practice.
118
==PAGE:131==
Tiny Bubbles in a Larger Industrial Context
Mass transfer from gas phases to liquid phases and the dispersion of bubbles into liq-
uids are the basis for many unit operations in chemical engineering.[57] So Norlex is
not the only company which could beneﬁt from the advancing technology of micro-
and nano-bubbles. Micro- and nano-bubbles can potentially be used in these three
industries: 1) the detergent industry, 2) the pharmaceutical industry, and 3) the food
industry.
When it comes to detergents, the general idea is that these become partially absorbed
on the surface of the bubble. Since the bubbles are so small they create a larger contact
area between the detergent and the dirt (larger surface-area-to-volume). And thus they
may provide a more energy-eﬃcient cleaning process.[125]
Within the pharmaceutical industry, the bubbles can e.g. be used for drug delivery.
Bubbles containing a drug is introduced into the body and move to the infected site.
Doing so the drug manufactures may wish to achieve a high concentration and an ef-
fective transfer of medical preparation to the diseased area. The amount of medication
being distributed throughout the rest of the body should be limited, thereby leading
to fewer side eﬀects. Moreover, the bubbles can be used as angiography1 or ultrasound
contrast agents. This is because the bubbles can easily move through the small vessels
of the body and the bubbles have a non-linear response to ultrasound.[125, 50] In the
future bubble injectors might replace the traditional vaccination injecting syringes. An
injector with small nozzles may be able to spray the bubbles with the vaccine directly
onto the skin. The bubbles will immediately penetrate the skin surface causing no
particular pain, wounding or transfer of undesirable bacteria.
Last but not least the bubbles can be used to enhance the organoleptic qualities of
foods.
By using micro- and/or nano-bubbles one can achieve a thicker and more
creamy mayonnaise. In addition, the bubbles can potentially accelerate the growth
of plants, shellﬁsh, and oysters. All of this is because the bubbles provide a better and
more eﬃcient aeration process than otherwise achieved.[3, 54, 61]
1A medical imaging technique used to visualise blood and lymph vessels. It is carried out after introduction of a
radiopaque substance.[126]
119
==PAGE:132==
Conclusion
The project was based on a speciﬁc research and development problem at Norlex. The
main objectives of this project were ﬁrstly to ﬁnd and describe existing documented
knowledge on bubble ozonation processes. Secondly, to assist Norlex in obtaining a
better mathematical understanding of the newly developed technology of micro- and
nano-bubbles. Thirdly to document and explore dynamic phenomena associated with
nano- and micro-bubble mass transfer.
The ﬁrst step was to ﬁnd and document all existing knowledge regarding ozone treat-
ment in wastewater processes and bubble systems. This literature study also helped
to gain a better understanding of the development challenges and opportunities that
Norlex are facing.
Ozone has many chemical properties that can be very useful in wastewater treatment.
It is a strong oxidising agent, able to help remove both inorganic, organic, and bio-
logical contaminants. It breaks down more diﬃcult contaminants, such as pesticides
and detergents, and makes PAH more bio-degradable. Additionally, it eﬀectively kills
bacteria, by destroying their cell walls, and viruses by attacking their RNA. So ozone
is well suited for disinfection processes or for pre-ozonation prior to the secondary
wastewater treatment. However, ozone also has some properties, which makes it dif-
ﬁcult to apply in wastewater treatment. It has a low solubility in water, a slow mass
transfer, and a fast decomposition rate. By using micro- and nano-bubbles (with larger
surface-to-volume ratios) in directing ozone into the water, this may oﬀset these bad
properties. So this method should provide a better mass transfer. Furthermore, the
residence time for tiny bubbles in the water will be longer than for larger bubbles,
which is also beneﬁcial. Diﬀusion between two diﬀerent phases - a gas phase and an
aqueous phase - is very complex and involves many diﬀerent ﬂow mechanisms. Usually,
theories such as the ”Two Film Theory” or ”The Surface Renewal Theory” are used
to describe the diﬀusion in such cases.
After the literature study the next step was to develop a mathematical model to sim-
ulate the diﬀusion process. Through an in-depth analysis and comparison of diﬀer-
ent mathematical models the most suitable model for describing the diﬀusion process
turned out to be a single-ﬁlm version of Model 3. This model both accounts for bubble
shrinkage and changes in the bulk gas phase concentration. The shrinkage of a bubble
is predicted to accelerate the ﬂux. So the higher the percentage of ozone in the bubble
the faster the mass transfer rate. A larger mole fraction of ozone will additionally
120
==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 17. CONCLUSION
result in a higher degree of non-linearity of Model 3. Therefore, Model 3 cannot be
simpliﬁed to a linear model.
A comprehensive sensitivity analysis showed that Model 3 complied with the general
concepts of diﬀusion. It also showed that R3 is the parameter that most strongly af-
fects the rate of diﬀusion and the overall amount of ozone transferred. A change in the
temperature, the pressure or the radius of the bulk gas phase do not really aﬀect the
rate at which the diﬀusion proceeds.
The third step was to establish a solid method for generating tiny bubbles. The ex-
perimental work in this project shows that it is possible to generate micro- and/or
nano-bubbles using a simple experimental set-up based on a decompression method.
It was also easy to demonstrate that the water is properly ozonated by a simple col-
orimetric test with DPD.
The fourth step was to determine diﬀerent model parameters with greater accuracy
through another literature survey.
Firstly, the diﬀusivity in the gas ﬁlm is 1.45 ·
10−5m2/s, which is much larger than the diﬀusivity in the liquid ﬁlm. A single-ﬁlm
model can be applied because the mass transfer resistance mainly lies in the liquid
ﬁlm.
For stagnant bubbles, theoretical Sherwood correlations predict that the liquid ﬁlm
thickness is half the size of the bubble.
The size distribution of the bubbles generated by Norlex’s bubble set-up was success-
fully determined using a particle size analyser. Based on ﬁve individual experiments
the average bubble size was determined to be 50µm. It was not very important what
software settings were used in the experiments regarding the stirrer speed, the duration
of measurement, and the degas function.
The ﬁnal step was to simulate a more realistic mass transfer case. This ﬁnal simula-
tion showed that the mass transfer from the micro-bubbles theoretically was extremely
rapid. The lifetime of a 50µm bubble was 136ms, while the time required to complete
the mass transfer through the liquid ﬁlm was 306ms.
Maybe the most important conclusion from this project is that the micro-bubbles pro-
duced by Norlex has great potential for being used eﬀectively in disinfection processes,
which is usually limited only by the rate of the mass transfer.
This master thesis has been a good learning process in how to study diﬀusion pro-
cesses and to describe such transport phenomena mathematically. Furthermore, the
project has provided a much greater understanding of the development of discretization
schemes and systems of ordinary diﬀerential equations. It has also been instructive to
carry out advanced simulations and designing GUI applications in MatLab.
121
==PAGE:134==
Suggestions for Further Work
The project work was limited to ﬁve months. If more time had been available then
some of the following suggestions may have been carried out. The suggestions aim at
improving some of the short-comings, which was uncovered during the project work.
18.1
Literature Survey
• Make a detailed study of the capital costs and maintenance expenditures associ-
ated with a typical ozonation system. Go deeper into why ozone so far has not
been competitive with available alternatives for the disinfection process.
• Make a full account of the optimal ozone concentrations in the pre-ozonation stage
and in the disinfection stage, based on other researchers’ experiences.
18.2
Model Development
• Include the impact of the increasing surface curvature of the shrinking bubble in
the model.
• Simulate a system, where the concentration in the bulk liquid phase is not constant
at 0mol/m3. Instead ozone should accumulate in the surrounding liquid.
• Include a surfactant shell in the model, so that the entire system consists of a
bulk gas phase, a liquid ﬁlm and a surfactant shell.
• Develop a mathematical model describing the diﬀusion process from surface bub-
bles1. For this purpose, models with a completely diﬀerent geometry and a zero
ﬂux condition at the interface between the bubble and the wall, should be devel-
oped.
• Set-up a model, which takes into account the upward movement of the bubbles.
One can then examine how the velocity of the moving bubble and the surrounding
water ﬂow aﬀect the ﬁlm thickness, and hence the mass transport.
• Develop multiple-bubble models, where the bubbles are not monodisperse but
vary in size. In this case the size distribution over time could be described using
a population-balance approach.
1Gas-ﬁlled pockets resting on a surface in the shape of an spherical caps.
122
==PAGE:135==
18.3. EXPERIMENTAL WORK
CHAPTER 18. SUGGESTIONS FOR FURTHER WORK
• Set up a much more advanced model in CFD software to describe the full experi-
mental situation in the tub with bubbles. The model should include the following
factors: 1) the diﬀusion of ozone from the bubbles to the bulk liquid, 2) the
buoyancy eﬀects, and 3) the decomposition of ozone.
• Use the diﬀusion theory and the mathematics developed in this project on a
diﬀerent case than ozonation in wastewater treatments.
• Provide a better implementation of the mathematical models in an open source
language with corresponding program. This means that companies like Norlex
would not have to rely on expensive license-based programs like MatLab.
18.3
Experimental Work
• See if it is possible experimentally to determine the ozone diﬀusion from the
bubbles. This was actually attempted in this project. However, there were too
many limitations related to the measuring equipment available.
• Make a more precise experimental study of the bubble lifetime.
• Determine the amount of bubbles generated in the water. This may be done by
ﬁlling bubble ozonated water into a beaker and observe the volume change when
the bubbles have disappeared form the liquid.
• Consider whether it is realistic to implement Norlex’s existing bubble system in
large treatment plants. This may be evaluated based on experiments on a pilot
scale.

==PAGE:154==
Explanation of the Dimensionless Groups
Appendix D is a brief description of the dimensionless groups commonly used to es-
timate the mass transfer coeﬃcients.
The ﬁrst dimensionless group is the Sherwood number (Sh), which is is given by:
Sh = kl · l
D
(D.1)
The Sherwood number is the ratio between the convective mass transfer and the diﬀu-
sive mass transfer. The higher the Sherwood number the higher the rate of convective
mass transfer relative to the rate of diﬀusion.[129] Dimensional analysis indicates that
the Sherwood number is a function of the Reynolds number (Re) and the Schmidt
number (Sc)[130]:
Sh = f(Re,Sc)
(D.2)
The Reynolds number and the Schmidt number are deﬁned as follows:
Re = ρ · v · l
µ
Sc =
µ
ρ · D
(D.3)
The Reynolds number is a dimensionless quantity used to predict the ﬂuid ﬂow sit-
uations. Its magnitude gives an indication of the relative importance of inertial and
viscous forces in a ﬂuid system. The Schmidt number is the ratio between the viscous
diﬀusion rate and the molecular diﬀusion rate.[129]
142
==PAGE:155==
Mass Transfer Coeﬃcients
Appendix E is an overview mass transfer coeﬃcients for various theories.
Model

Linear Extrapolation
Appendix F explains in greater detail the ﬁrst attempt to establish a set of boundary
conditions at the gas-liquid-interface.
Generally, the task is to establish the right mathematical expressions (ODEs) for the
compartments on either side of the interface (cNi−1, cNi+1, and cNi+2).
Ozone Concentration in the Gas Film Right Next to the Interface
In this approach it is assumed that (rNi−2, cNi−2), (rNi−1, cNi−1), and (rNi, cNi) (see
Figure 7.4) are three points on a straight line following this linear equation:
c = dc
dr · r + β
(F.1)
Notice that in this approach it is assumed that the interface compartment, Ni, is a gas
ﬁlm compartment.
Now Equation (F.1) is written for the ﬁrst point on the line (rNi−2, cNi−2). This equation
is then used to determine an expression for the slope of the line
  dc
dr

between the three
points:
cNi−2 = dc
dr · rNi−2 + β ⇒dc
dr = cNi−2 −β
R1
(F.2)
The point (rNi−1, cNi−1) is now inserted in Equation (F.1). Furthermore, the expression
for the slope in Equation (F.2) is also inserted in Equation (F.1). Then the intercept,
β, is determined as follows:
cNi−1 = cNi−2 −β
R1
· rNi−1 + β ⇒β = cNi−1 · rNi−2 −cNi−2 · rNi−1
rNi−2 −rNi−1
(F.3)
Finally, the last point on the straight line (rNi, cNi) is inserted in Equation (F.1):
cNi = dc
dr · rNi + β
(F.4)
When the expressions for dc
dr and β are inserted in Equation (F.4) the following equation
is obtained:
cNi =
rNi −rNi−1
rNi−2 −rNi−1
· cNi−2 + cNi−1 · rNi−2 −rNi
rNi−2 −rNi−1
(F.5)

APPENDIX F. LINEAR EXTRAPOLATION
Equation (F.5) can be rewritten using ∆r.
Furthermore, cNi can be isolated in the
equation. This leads to:
∆r
−∆r · cNi−2 + cNi−1 ·

−∆r

⇒cNi = −cNi−2 + 2 · cNi−1
(F.6)
The expression for cNi given by Equation (F.6) can now be inserted into Equation (7.30).
This gives the following ODE for compartment Ni −1:
cNi−1
dt
= Dgf ·
2 · cNi−2
∆r2
+ 2cNi−1
rNi−1∆r

(F.7)
Now an ODE for the gas ﬁlm compartment adjacent to the interface has been established.
The next step is to set up mathematical expressions for the liquid ﬁlm compartments
next to the interface.
Ozone Concentration in the Liquid Film Right Next to the Interface
There will be a phase transition from gas to liquid between compartment Ni-1 and Ni+1.
The phase transition does not happen between compartment Ni and Ni+1 due to the fact
that Ni is not an actual compartment in the system. Ni is only a ﬁctive compartment.
There are several ways of describing the solubility of a gas in water. In this case it is
assumed that ozone obeys Henry’s law. So the amount of ozone which is transferred
from compartment Ni-1 to the ﬁrst liquid ﬁlm compartment, Ni+1, is given by:
cNi+1 = Hcc · cNi−1
(F.8)
Equation (F.8) is an explicit expression for the concentration in the ﬁrst liquid ﬁlm com-
partment.
The concentration of ozone in the second liquid ﬁlm compartment is calculated using
the following ODE:
dcNi+2
dt
= Dlf·

cNi+1 ·
 1
∆r2 −
1
rNi+2 · ∆r

−cNi+2 ·
 2
∆r2

+ cNi+3 ·
 1
∆r2 +
1
rNi+2 · ∆r

(F.9)
Now all the mathematical expressions for the compartments adjacent to the gas-liquid-
interface has been described for the ﬁrst approach.

function createOutput(app, event)

46
f = uifigure;

uidlg = uiprogressdlg(f,'Title','Please Wait','Message','Opening the ...
application');
48
pause(.5)
49
50
d = struct(...

'N',
app.NEditField.Value,
... % Number of discretizations + 2
52

app.DgfEditField.Value, ... % Diffusion coefficient in the gas film

app.DlfEditField.Value,
... % Diffusion coefficient in the ...
liquid film
54
'c0',
app.c0EditField.Value,
... % Start concentration in the gas film

'clf',
app.clfEditField.Value,
... % Start concentration in the liquid film
56

app.HccEditField.Value,
... % Henry's constant
57

app.R1EditField.Value,
... % The radius of the gas bubble
58
'R3',
app.R3EditField.Value,
... % The radius of the liquid film
59
'q',
app.qEditField.Value,
... % the ratio between the compartments ...
on either side of the interface
60
'opt',odeset('AbsTol',1e-3,'RelTol',1e-3) ... ODE options
61
);
62
63
% The length of the two layers of film
64
d.l
= d.R3 - d.R1;
65
66
d.R2 = d.l / 2 + d.R1;
67
68
% Running calculation
69
uidlg.Value = .33;
70
uidlg.Message = 'Running calculation...';
71
pause(1)
72
73
d = initstructModel1(d);
74
75
%Specify the simulation time
76
if app.t0DropDown.Value == "[1e-7 2e-7 3e-7 1e-6 2e-6 3e-6 4e-6 5e-6 1e-5 ...
2e-5 3e-5 1e-4 2e-4 3e-4]"
77
t0 = [1e-7 2e-7 3e-7 1e-6 2e-6 3e-6 4e-6 5e-6 1e-5 2e-5 3e-5 1e-4 2e-4 ...
3e-4];
78
elseif app.t0DropDown.Value == "linspace(0,3,10000)"

t0 = linspace(0,3,10000);

elseif app.t0DropDown.Value == "linspace(0,3,1000)"

==PAGE:160==
APPENDIX G. MATLAB CODE TO MODEL 1
81
t0 = linspace(0,3,1000);

elseif app.t0DropDown.Value == "[0 .0000005 .000001 .000002 .000005 .00001 ...
.00003 .0001 .0004 .0005 .001 .0015 .002 .003 .005]"

t0 = [0 .0000005 .000001 .000002 .000005 .00001 .00003 .0001 .0004 ...
.0005 .001 .0015 .002 .003 .005];

elseif app.t0DropDown.Value == "[0 .003 0.03 0.05 0.10 0.20 0.30 2]"

91

92
93
% The interface concentration of the gas film
94
CNigf = (d.Dlf .* y(:, d.Nc1 + 1) + d.Dgf .* y(:, d.Nc1)) ./ (d.Hcc * d.Dlf ...
+ d.Dgf);
95
96
% The interface concentration of the liquid film

CNilf = d.Hcc .* CNigf;
98

109

118

[tsteady, tsteadyliquid, YY, YYliquid] = CalculateParameters(d, y, t);

app.SteadystateconcentrationinthegasfilmEditField.Value = string(YY);

app.SteadystateconcentrationintheliquidfilmEditField.Value = string(YYliquid);

function dcdt = DiffusionModel1(t,c,d)
3
% This function is used to solve the differential equation
4
5
dcdt = d.A*c;
6
7
% The boundary at N = 1:
8
dcdt(1) = dcdt(1) + d.Dgf * d.c0 * ((1/d.Dxˆ2) - (1/((d.r(1))*d.Dx)));
9
10
% % The boundary at N:
11
dcdt(d.N-2) = dcdt(d.N-2) + d.Dlf * 0 * ((1/d.Dxˆ2) + (1/((d.r(d.N-2))*d.Dx)));
initstructModel1.m
1
2
function d = initstructModel1(d)
3
% This function initialises the data structure for the diffusion problem.
4
% Furthermore, it is used to build the A-matrix.
5
6
%% Separation into Compartments
7
% Interface number
8
if (mod((d.N-2)*d.q,1) == 0)
9
d.Nc1 = (d.N-2)*d.q;
% The compartments on the gas side of the interface
10
d.Nc2 = (d.N-2)-d.Nc1;
% The compartments on the liquid side of the interface
11
d.Nigf = d.Nc1 + 1;
% Define the position of the first interface
12
d.Nilf = d.Nc1 + 2;
% Define the position of the second interface
13
else
14
error('d.N should be an equal number! Or the value of k should be changed');
15
end
16
17
% Making of Delta r:
18
d.Dx = d.l ./ (d.N-2);
19
20
% The outer radius of the individual compartments
21
d.r = ones(d.N-2,1);
22
for i = 1:d.N-2
23
d.r(i) = d.R1 + d.Dx + d.Dx*(i-1);
24
end
25
26
%% Generate the A Matrix
27
28
% Gas side
29
% Corresponding to 1/Delta rˆ2 * D:
30
d.c1gf = d.Dgf ./ (d.Dx.ˆ2);
31
32
d.r1 = (1 ./ d.Dxˆ2).*ones(d.Nc1,1);
33
d.r2 = 1 ./ (d.r .* d.Dx);
34
35
% Corresponding to (1/Delta rˆ2) + (1/ri * Delta rˆ2):
36
d.c2gf = d.Dgf .* (d.r1(1:d.Nc1-1)+d.r2(1:d.Nc1-1));
37
38
% Corresponding to (1/Delta rˆ2) - (1/ri * Delta rˆ2):
39
d.c3gf = d.Dgf .* (d.r1(2:d.Nc1) - d.r2(2:d.Nc1));
40
41
% The gas part of the matrix:
42
d.Agf = -2 .* d.c1gf .* eye(d.Nc1) + diag(d.c2gf,1) + diag(d.c3gf,-1);
43
44
% Liquid side
153
==PAGE:166==
APPENDIX G. MATLAB CODE TO MODEL 1
45
% Corresponding to 1/Delta rˆ2 * D:
46
d.c1lf = d.Dlf ./ (d.Dx.ˆ2);
47
48
d.r3 = (1 ./ d.Dxˆ2).*ones(d.Nc2,1);
49
d.r4 = 1 ./ (d.r((d.Nc1+1):end) .* d.Dx);
50
51
% Corresponding to (1/Delta rˆ2) + (1/ri * Delta rˆ2):
52
d.c2lf = d.Dlf .* (d.r3(1:d.Nc2-1)+d.r4(1:d.Nc2-1));
53
54
% Corresponding to (1/Delta rˆ2) - (1/ri * Delta rˆ2):
55
d.c3lf = d.Dlf .* (d.r3(2:d.Nc2) - d.r4(2:d.Nc2));
56
57
% The liquid part of the matrix:
58
d.Alf = -2 .* d.c1lf .* eye(d.Nc2) + diag(d.c2lf,1) + diag(d.c3lf,-1);
59
60
% The entire matrix:
61
W1 = zeros(d.Nc2,d.Nc1);
62
d.Agf = [d.Agf; W1];
63
W2 = zeros(d.Nc1,d.Nc2);
64
d.Alf = [W2; d.Alf];
65
d.A = [d.Agf d.Alf];
66
67
68
%% The Interface Boundaries
69
70
% Variables:
71
var1 = ((1/d.Dxˆ2) - (1/(d.r(d.Nc1)*d.Dx)));
72
var11 = ((1/d.Dxˆ2) + (1/(d.r(d.Nc1)*d.Dx)));
73
var2 = (-2/d.Dxˆ2);
74
var3 = (d.Hcc * d.Dlf + d.Dgf);
75
76
% At Ni-1
77
d.A(d.Nc1,d.Nc1-1) = d.Dgf*var1;
78
d.A(d.Nc1,d.Nc1) = d.Dgf*(var2 + d.Dgf*(var11/var3));
79
d.A(d.Nc1,d.Nc1+1) = d.Dgf*(d.Dlf * (var11/var3));
80
%
81
% Variables:
82
var4 = ((1/d.Dxˆ2) + (1/(d.r(d.Nc1+1)*d.Dx)));
83
var5 = ((1/d.Dxˆ2) - (1/(d.r(d.Nc1+1)*d.Dx)));
84
var6 = d.Hcc*d.Dlf;
85
var7 = d.Hcc*d.Dgf;
86
87
% At Ni+1
88
d.A(d.Nc1+1,d.Nc1+2) = d.Dlf* var4;
89
d.A(d.Nc1+1,d.Nc1+1) = d.Dlf*(var6*(var5/var3)+var2);
90
d.A(d.Nc1+1,d.Nc1) = d.Dlf*(var7 * (var5/var3));

The MatLab Application
Appendix H shows the layout of the Graphical User Interface for the MatLab applica-
tion.
Figure H.1: The GUI when the user has typed in all the parameters and pressed ’Create Output’.
As seen in Figure H.1 the user can type in all the model input parameters. The GUI also
makes it possible to choose between diﬀerent types of time settings from a drop-down
162
==PAGE:175==
APPENDIX H. THE MATLAB APPLICATION
menu. The user can either select a time vector with randomly distributed points in
time or use ’linspace’ with evenly spaced points in time. It is more appropriate to use
’linspace’ for plotting the concentration as a function of time. A randomly spaced time
vector should be used when plotting the concentration as a function of radius and for
the movie feature.
Figure H.2: The GUI when the output has been generated.
Figure H.2 shows how plots are generated and the steady state outputs are printed out
once the application has ﬁnished loading.
163
